"14535"	"he"	"explains"	" Connery is dressed as if he has just strolled in from a golf course -- slacks, sport shoes, gray sport shirt with sleeves rolled up to reveal barely legible tattoos on his right forearm (they say ``Scotland Forever'' and ``Mum &AMP; Dad''), gray sweater tied around his neck. Although he is fighting jet lag, having flown from his home in Marbella, Spain, the interview begins precisely on time. Part of the Connery mystique stems from his air of panache, romantic brooding and a sense that he plays for keeps. But he cut short a youthful hitch in the British navy (where he acquired the tattoos) because of ulcers. No stranger to anxiety, he is a total pro who always shows up prepared and expects others to be. He is not casual about things going wrong. Hence, he explains, the producing. ``I've been doing it for 20 years,'' he says, grinning a lopsided grin, ``ever since I've had actor, director and script approval. If you don't control the blueprint of what the film's really about, it very easily slips away. </P> "	"nyt_eng_199502.gz"

(NYT19) NEW BRUNSWICK, N.J. -- February 15, 1995 -- RUTGERS-STUDENTS, 2-15 -- Many Rutgers students were angered by statements by the university president, published this month, that disadvantaged students fared poorly on college admissions tests because of their ``genetic hereditary background.'' Last week, demonstrators asked for the resignation of the president, Francis L. Lawrence. (Norman Y. Lono/New York Times Photo)   (NYT18) BERKELEY, Calif. -- February 15, 1995 -- CALIF-AFFIRM-ACTION-2, 2-15 -- Glynn Custred, foreground, and Tom Woods are leading a campaign to repeal California's affirmative action laws. Last weekend, several minority law school students were the targets of racial hate mail aimed at them and at several professors because these academics claimed they were only at the law school as a result of affirmative action.. (Jim Wilson/New York Times Photo)   (NYT17) RALEIGH, N.C. -- February 15, 1995 -- COMPUTER-HACKER-NEMESIS, 2-15 -- Tsutomu Shimomura, 30, is a computational physicist with a reputation as a brilliant cyber-sleuth in the tightly knit community of programmers and engineers who defend the country's computer networks. And he is on the trail of Kevin D. Mitnick, the hacker behind a recent spree of break-ins to dozens of corporate, university and personal computers on the global internet. (Alan Decker/New York Times Photo)   (NYT16) DUESSELDORF, Germany -- February 15, 1995 -- EUROPE-TAXES, 2-15 -- With taxes rising in most of Europe, taxpayers are grumbling, but most are willing to pay. Johann Scholtz, seen in his violin maker's workshop in Duesseldorf, says a levy to rebuild eastern Germany is worthwhile. ``I don't know how much I pay,'' says the 55-year-old master violin maker. ``And the depressing thing is, my taxes are going up again this year.'' (Karin Hill/New York Times Photo)   (NYT15) WASHINGTON -- February 15, 1995 -- CONGRESS-WELFARE, 2-15 -- Ron Haskins, staff director of the House Ways and Means Subcommittee on Human Resources, during a break in the panel's deliberations before it cleared a plan to overhaul the federal welfare system. The sweeping legislation thst is expected to pass will cut off cash assistance to hundreds of thousands of children with physical or mental disabilities. (Stephen Crowley/New York Times Photo)   (NYT14) BUFFALO, N.Y. -- February 15, 1995 -- POLICE-CHIEFS-CRIME, 2-15 -- Many police departments oppose Republican efforts to alter the crime bill signed by President Clinton last year. In Buffalo, Commissioner R. Gil Kerlikowske, right, planned to use some of the bill's money to replace Officer John Bartholomew, left, with a secretary, allowing the officer to take a street beat. (Keith Meyers/New York Times Photo)   (NYT13) BERKELEY, Calif. -- February 15, 1995 -- CALIF-AFFIRM-ACTION-1, 2-15 -- Law students at UC Berkeley's Boalt Hall listen to speakers who decried racial hate mail at a rally Wednesday. Last weekend, several minority law school students were the targets of racial hate mail aimed at them and at several professors whom an academic - one of two such ``staunch conservatives'' - claimed were only at the law school as a result of affirmative action. (Jim Wilson/New York Times Photo)   (NYT12) DAYTONA BEACH, Fla. -- February 15, 1995 -- DAYTONA-WOMAN-NYTRENG-STANDALONE-CLR, 2-15 -- Ford Thunderbird driver Shawna Robinson stands in the garage area of the Daytona International Speedway prior to qualifying Wednesday for the 37th Daytona 500, to be held on Sunday, Feb. 19. The Des Moines, Iowa native is the only woman attempting to make the starting field of the Daytona 500. (Tony Ranze/Lakeland Ledger Photo)   (NYT11) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- ADV. (New York Times Photo)   (NYT10) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- PEPIN-COOKING-BOS-CLR, 2-15 -- In Jacques Pepin's 15th book, ``Jacques Pepin's Simple and Healthy Cooking,'' primitive, brightly colored borders of vegetables and flowers open each chapter and edge the pages. The healthy aspect of this new book, he explains, is that he uses low proportions of butter, beef and sugar, ``and larger amounts of other, more healthful ingredients in my recipes.'' Pepin is shown with his old friend and master chef, Julia Child (Pam Berry/Boston Globe Photo)   (NYT9) BOSTON -- February 15, 1995 -- KELMAN-PROFILE-BOS-CLR, 2-15 -- James Kelman's fifth novel, ``How Late It Was, How Late,'' won the 1994 Booker Prize, Britain's top literary prize, which roused the dozing lions of respectable letters. ``It is my right to be a writer,'' says Kelman, who makes a case ``for the validity of any culture, any language. It wasn't a case of saying my culture is better than yours, but that mine is not any worse than yours.'' Kelman is shown in Boston, Mass., recently. (Lane Turner/Boston Globe Photo)   (NYT8) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- SHOPPING-INCENTIVE-BOS-CLR, 2-15 -- A+ America Inc. bills its mission as the old S&AMP;H green stamps idea with a noble twist, setting aside a portion of sales to buy new technology for schools. After deciding on a computer nearly a year ago, the Lynn English High School in Massachussetts settled on a $679 laser-jet printer. ``If we didn't get something soon, people might wonder whether it was worthwhile or not,'' math department head Mary Lou Cahalane said of A+ America. (Fred R. Conrad/New York Times Photo)   (NYT5) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- PRATT-PIANIST-PROFILE, 2-15 -- Awadagin Pratt, 28, is not your garden-variety, prize-winning young pianist. Besides his low-slung piano bench and his casual-looking clothes, there are his dreadlocks. And there is the fact that makes him a rarity in the mostly-white world of classical music: he is black. Pratt is shown preparing for a performance at High Point High School in Beltsville, Md., as part of his Outreach program. (Cynthia Johnson/New York Times Photo)   (NYT4) FAIRFAX, Va. -- February 15, 1995 -- TV-SWITCH-OFF, 2-15 -- Laela Shallal shows her first grade class a clay fish she made with her father in her TV-free home. On average, according to data gathered by the A.C. Neilsen Co., preschool children watch 30 hours of television a week; experts claim more than 10 hours a week can have a detrimental effect on childrens' attention span and communications skills. (Marty Katz/New York Times Photo)   (NYT3) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- ARTIST-SUICIDE, 2-12 -- Enigmatic artist Ray Johnson, who drowned in Sag Harbor, N.Y. on Jan. 13, in a photo taken by a friend. Ialacci, Sag Harbor's police chief. ``I've got to tell you, it's like doing a gigantic puzzle.'' (Katie Seiden/New York Times Photo)   (NYT2) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- HOME-IMPROVEMENT, 2-15 -- A picture frame is a structure, usually of wood or metal, that surrounds and supports the picture and isolates it from its surrounding. Water, pastels, prints and photographs should also be mounted on backing board, matted and glazed. Oil and acrylic painting should be framed, but they do not require mats or glass. (Edward R. On average, according to data gathered by the A.C. Neilsen Co., preschool children watch 30 hours of television a week; experts claim more than 10 hours a week can have a detrimental effect on childrens' attention span and communications skills. (Marty Katz/New York Times Photo)   (NYT3) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- ARTIST-SUICIDE, 2-12 -- Enigmatic artist Ray Johnson, who drowned in Sag Harbor, N.Y. on Jan. 13, in a photo taken by a friend. His death remains a mystery. ``This is one of the strangest cases I've ever been involved in,'' says Joseph J. Ialacci, Sag Harbor's police chief. ``I've got to tell you, it's like doing a gigantic puzzle.'' (Katie Seiden/New York Times Photo)   (NYT2) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- HOME-IMPROVEMENT, 2-15 -- A picture frame is a structure, usually of wood or metal, that surrounds and supports the picture and isolates it from its surrounding. Water, pastels, prints and photographs should also be mounted on backing board, matted and glazed. Oil and acrylic painting should be framed, but they do not require mats or glass. (Edward R. Lipinski/New York Times Illustration)   (NYT1) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- HOMER-STOLEN-PAINTING, 2-14 -- ``Off Gloucester Harbor,'' an 1880 watercolor by Winslow Homer that was stolen in 1969. It was returned to its owner Tuesday, with the addition of a sailboat and some sea gulls (to the left of the rowboat). (NYT19) NEW BRUNSWICK, N.J. -- February 15, 1995 -- RUTGERS-STUDENTS, 2-15 -- Many Rutgers students were angered by statements by the university president, published this month, that disadvantaged students fared poorly on college admissions tests because of their ``genetic hereditary background.'' Last week, demonstrators asked for the resignation of the president, Francis L. Lawrence. (Norman Y. Lono/New York Times Photo)   (NYT18) BERKELEY, Calif. -- February 15, 1995 -- CALIF-AFFIRM-ACTION-2, 2-15 -- Glynn Custred, foreground, and Tom Woods are leading a campaign to repeal California's affirmative action laws. Last weekend, several minority law school students were the targets of racial hate mail aimed at them and at several professors because these academics claimed they were only at the law school as a result of affirmative action.. (Jim Wilson/New York Times Photo)   (NYT17) RALEIGH, N.C. -- February 15, 1995 -- COMPUTER-HACKER-NEMESIS, 2-15 -- Tsutomu Shimomura, 30, is a computational physicist with a reputation as a brilliant cyber-sleuth in the tightly knit community of programmers and engineers who defend the country's computer networks. And he is on the trail of Kevin D. Mitnick, the hacker behind a recent spree of break-ins to dozens of corporate, university and personal computers on the global internet. (Alan Decker/New York Times Photo)   (NYT16) DUESSELDORF, Germany -- February 15, 1995 -- EUROPE-TAXES, 2-15 -- With taxes rising in most of Europe, taxpayers are grumbling, but most are willing to pay. Johann Scholtz, seen in his violin maker's workshop in Duesseldorf, says a levy to rebuild eastern Germany is worthwhile. ``I don't know how much I pay,'' says the 55-year-old master violin maker. ``And the depressing thing is, my taxes are going up again this year.'' (Karin Hill/New York Times Photo)   (NYT15) WASHINGTON -- February 15, 1995 -- CONGRESS-WELFARE, 2-15 -- Ron Haskins, staff director of the House Ways and Means Subcommittee on Human Resources, during a break in the panel's deliberations before it cleared a plan to overhaul the federal welfare system. The sweeping legislation thst is expected to pass will cut off cash assistance to hundreds of thousands of children with physical or mental disabilities. (Stephen Crowley/New York Times Photo)   (NYT14) BUFFALO, N.Y. -- February 15, 1995 -- POLICE-CHIEFS-CRIME, 2-15 -- Many police departments oppose Republican efforts to alter the crime bill signed by President Clinton last year. In Buffalo, Commissioner R. Gil Kerlikowske, right, planned to use some of the bill's money to replace Officer John Bartholomew, left, with a secretary, allowing the officer to take a street beat. (Keith Meyers/New York Times Photo)   (NYT13) BERKELEY, Calif. -- February 15, 1995 -- CALIF-AFFIRM-ACTION-1, 2-15 -- Law students at UC Berkeley's Boalt Hall listen to speakers who decried racial hate mail at a rally Wednesday. Last weekend, several minority law school students were the targets of racial hate mail aimed at them and at several professors whom an academic - one of two such ``staunch conservatives'' - claimed were only at the law school as a result of affirmative action. (Jim Wilson/New York Times Photo)   (NYT12) DAYTONA BEACH, Fla. -- February 15, 1995 -- DAYTONA-WOMAN-NYTRENG-STANDALONE-CLR, 2-15 -- Ford Thunderbird driver Shawna Robinson stands in the garage area of the Daytona International Speedway prior to qualifying Wednesday for the 37th Daytona 500, to be held on Sunday, Feb. 19. The Des Moines, Iowa native is the only woman attempting to make the starting field of the Daytona 500. (Tony Ranze/Lakeland Ledger Photo)   (NYT11) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- ADV. FOR SUN., FEB. 19 -- ABOUT-CARS-LUXURY-SEDANS, 2-15 -- Lincoln Continental, Cadillac SLS and Chrysler LHS: America's reborn luxury sedans. The Continental, top: Newfound power and technology; The Cadillac: Angular, masculine and conservative; The Chrysler, bottom: High style at a relatively attractive price. (New York Times Photo)   (NYT10) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- PEPIN-COOKING-BOS-CLR, 2-15 -- In Jacques Pepin's 15th book, ``Jacques Pepin's Simple and Healthy Cooking,'' primitive, brightly colored borders of vegetables and flowers open each chapter and edge the pages. The healthy aspect of this new book, he explains, is that he uses low proportions of butter, beef and sugar, ``and larger amounts of other, more healthful ingredients in my recipes.'' Pepin is shown with his old friend and master chef, Julia Child (Pam Berry/Boston Globe Photo)   (NYT9) BOSTON -- February 15, 1995 -- KELMAN-PROFILE-BOS-CLR, 2-15 -- James Kelman's fifth novel, ``How Late It Was, How Late,'' won the 1994 Booker Prize, Britain's top literary prize, which roused the dozing lions of respectable letters. ``It is my right to be a writer,'' says Kelman, who makes a case ``for the validity of any culture, any language. It wasn't a case of saying my culture is better than yours, but that mine is not any worse than yours.'' Kelman is shown in Boston, Mass., recently. (Lane Turner/Boston Globe Photo)   (NYT8) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- SHOPPING-INCENTIVE-BOS-CLR, 2-15 -- A+ America Inc. bills its mission as the old S&AMP;H green stamps idea with a noble twist, setting aside a portion of sales to buy new technology for schools. After deciding on a computer nearly a year ago, the Lynn English High School in Massachussetts settled on a $679 laser-jet printer. ``If we didn't get something soon, people might wonder whether it was worthwhile or not,'' math department head Mary Lou Cahalane said of A+ America. (Barry Chin/Boston Globe Photo)   (NYT7) READSBORO, Vt. -- February 15, 1995 -- HOUSE-PROUD, 2-15 -- In the new book ``A Year at North Hill,'' in which poetry is perfectly at home with the eminently practical matters of gardening, Wayne Winterrowd and his partner, Joe Eck, recount in glorious detail all four seasons in their Vermont garden. Winterrowd, left, and Eck shown in front of their home and garden. (Paul O. Boisvert/New York Times Photo)   (NYT6) NEW YORK -- February 15, 1995 -- TOILET-BRUSHES, 2-15 -- Like a knight of old, the French designer Philippe Starck brandishes Excalibur (his new design for a toilet brush and holder). ``It was a product I wanted to do for 20 years,'' said Starck, 46. ``I've always thought it would be the apotheosis of my career.'' (Fred R. Conrad/New York Times Photo)   (NYT5) UNDATED -- February 15, 1995 -- PRATT-PIANIST-PROFILE, 2-15 -- Awadagin Pratt, 28, is not your garden-variety, prize-winning young pianist. Besides his low-slung piano bench and his casual-looking clothes, there are his dreadlocks. And there is the fact that makes him a rarity in the mostly-white world of classical music: he is black. Pratt is shown preparing for a performance at High Point High School in Beltsville, Md., as part of his Outreach program. The listings are based on computer-processed sales figures from 3,050 bookstores and from representative wholesalers with more than 38,000 retail outlets, including newsstands, variety stores, supermarkets, and bookstores. These figures are statistically adjusted to represent sales in all such outlets across the United States. The number of titles within the two subdivisions of non-fiction can change from week to week, reflecting changes in book buying.  An `x' following a book's ranking indicates that its sales, weighted to reflect the book-selling industry nationally, are barely distinguishable from those of the book above. (HLW) By LAUREL GRAEBER c.1995 N.Y. Times News Service  PARALLEL TIME: Growing Up in Black and White  By Brent Staples. Avon, $11.  The author, an editorial writer for The New York Times, recalls his experiences as the eldest son of a black family in Chester, Pa., a town where obvious forms of segregation had vanished but more subtle divisions between the races were strong. His memoir explores those boundaries and the one between himself and his brother Blake, a drug dealer who died violently. ``It is a complex portrait of a worldly education, written at times with affection, at times with a sting,'' Verlyn Klinkenborg wrote here last year.  A BOOK OF COMMON PRAYER  By Joan Didion. Vintage International, $12.  Charlotte Douglas, a woman with no understanding of revolutions, seeks her 18-year-old daughter, who is trying to foment one. After participating in a bombing and a hijacking, the young woman is believed to be in Boca Grande, a fictional Central American country. Grace Strasser-Mendana, another American expatriate, tells their story. In 1977, our reviewer, Joyce Carol Oates, said that Joan Didion ``has been an articulate witness to the most stubborn and intractable truths of our time, a memorable voice, partly eulogistic, partly despairing; always in control.'' Vintage has also brought another of Ms. Didion's novels back into print: DEMOCRACY ($12), in which the author herself is a character. She narrates the drama of the Victor family, whose life in 1975 involves both politics and murder. The author ``is wonderful not only at hearing her characters but at naming them,'' Mary McCarthy said in the Book Review in 1984.  LISTENING  By Hannah Merker. Harper Perennial, $12.  More than two decades ago, the author went skiing for the first time _ an event that marked the last time she would ever hear completely. Her book assesses the importance of sound and demonstrates how she adjusted to the silence imposed by a devastating accident. Writing in these pages last year, Lou Ann Walker said: ``We feel her loss. ... But we are receptive to the lessons of listening she is teaching us.'' Also reissued is TRAIN GO SORRY: Inside a Deaf World, (Vintage, $13). The author, Leah Hager Cohen, is the daughter of the superintendent of the Lexington School for the Deaf in New York. A hearing child of hearing parents, she describes her life within a culture she could never really be part of. In 1994, our reviewer, Russ Rymer, said the book often reads ``like a love story _ as intimate, tender and troubling a love story as any you're likely to find.''  THE WAY WE KNOW IN DREAMS: Stories  By Gordon Weaver. University of Missouri, $14.95.  Many of the men in this collection are disappointed middle-aged souls who feel powerless to change their fates _ as well they might, since two of the other characters are God and Satan. The result is ``inspired, clever, and unexpected fiction,'' Chris Bohjalian wrote here last year. The men in the stories in WANTING ONLY TO BE HEARD, by Jack Driscoll (University of Massachusetts, $12.95), are even more desperate. Frequently hunters and fishermen, often bereft of female company, they live close to nature _ and to violence. In 1993, Jonis Agee said in the Book Review that ``Jack Driscoll's poetic language gives them a stature that makes their struggle meaningful, tragic and universal.''  MOTHERLESS DAUGHTERS: The Legacy of Loss  By Hope Edelman. Delta, $12.95.  The author, who was 17 when her mother died, draws on clinical research and the experiences of more than 200 other women to analyze the impact of such a loss. Daughters in mourning, she explains, often feel that their own lives are incomplete. This is ``a moving and valuable treatment of a neglected subject,'' Nancy Caldwell Sorel said here last year. Maternal roles are also examined in THE MYTHS OF MOTHERHOOD: How Culture Reinvents the Good Mother, by Shari L. Thurer (Penguin, $12.95). Ranging from the Stone Age to today, this history reveals how society, not biology, has defined women's task as parents. In 1994, our reviewer, Rachel Billington, called it ``positive, sensible and readable.''  ACTS OF LOVE  By Emily Listfield. Penguin, $10.95.  It's a familiar scenario: a gun goes off in a suburban living room, and a marital quarrel ends in death. The question this novel poses is whether Ted Waring deliberately or accidentally killed his estranged wife. The author's ``chilling meditation ... is a sobering reminder that some puzzles are better left unsolved,'' Sarah Ferguson said in the Book Review last year.  THE PROTEAN SELF: Human Resilience in an Age of Fragmentation  By Robert Jay Lifton. Basic Books, $13.  If a foolish consistency is indeed the hobgoblin of little minds, the author has found quite a few large-minded people. Their response to the dislocation of today's world is to become eminently adaptable. In 1994, our reviewer, Richard A. Shweder, found this ``a fascinating and appealing book because it is a near-perfect example of an intellectual stance that skeptics sometimes disparage.'' Human resilience is also evident in THE AGELESS SELF: Sources of Meaning in Late Life, by Sharon R. Kaufman (University of Wisconsin, $11.95). A study of 60 elderly men and women, it demonstrates that strength of spirit and mind often compensates for bodily weakness. It is ``a book that no novelist or indeed any caring person with a fondness for human interiors could resist,'' Paul West said here in 1987.  DROWN ALL THE DOGS  By Thomas Adcock. Pocket Books, $5.50.  Detective Neil Hockaday has to confront his own family's shadowy past when he returns to Ireland to find out about the father he never knew. Last year, our reviewer, Marilyn Stasio, called this mystery's dialogue ``lively and literate.'' </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950507.0026 type=story > <HEADLINE> V-E DAY CELEBRATIONS TURN SOMBER IN BRITAIN </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> LONDON (BC-V-E-BRITAIN-500&AMP;add-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199505.gz"

(HLW) By LAUREL GRAEBER c.1995 N.Y. Times News Service  PARALLEL TIME: Growing Up in Black and White  By Brent Staples. Avon, $11.  The author, an editorial writer for The New York Times, recalls his experiences as the eldest son of a black family in Chester, Pa., a town where obvious forms of segregation had vanished but more subtle divisions between the races were strong. His memoir explores those boundaries and the one between himself and his brother Blake, a drug dealer who died violently. ``It is a complex portrait of a worldly education, written at times with affection, at times with a sting,'' Verlyn Klinkenborg wrote here last year.  A BOOK OF COMMON PRAYER  By Joan Didion. Vintage International, $12.  Charlotte Douglas, a woman with no understanding of revolutions, seeks her 18-year-old daughter, who is trying to foment one. After participating in a bombing and a hijacking, the young woman is believed to be in Boca Grande, a fictional Central American country. Grace Strasser-Mendana, another American expatriate, tells their story. In 1977, our reviewer, Joyce Carol Oates, said that Joan Didion ``has been an articulate witness to the most stubborn and intractable truths of our time, a memorable voice, partly eulogistic, partly despairing; always in control.'' Vintage has also brought another of Ms. Didion's novels back into print: DEMOCRACY ($12), in which the author herself is a character. She narrates the drama of the Victor family, whose life in 1975 involves both politics and murder. The author ``is wonderful not only at hearing her characters but at naming them,'' Mary McCarthy said in the Book Review in 1984.  LISTENING  By Hannah Merker. Harper Perennial, $12.  More than two decades ago, the author went skiing for the first time _ an event that marked the last time she would ever hear completely. Her book assesses the importance of sound and demonstrates how she adjusted to the silence imposed by a devastating accident. Writing in these pages last year, Lou Ann Walker said: ``We feel her loss. ... But we are receptive to the lessons of listening she is teaching us.'' Also reissued is TRAIN GO SORRY: Inside a Deaf World, (Vintage, $13). The author, Leah Hager Cohen, is the daughter of the superintendent of the Lexington School for the Deaf in New York. A hearing child of hearing parents, she describes her life within a culture she could never really be part of. In 1994, our reviewer, Russ Rymer, said the book often reads ``like a love story _ as intimate, tender and troubling a love story as any you're likely to find.''  THE WAY WE KNOW IN DREAMS: Stories  By Gordon Weaver. University of Missouri, $14.95.  Many of the men in this collection are disappointed middle-aged souls who feel powerless to change their fates _ as well they might, since two of the other characters are God and Satan. The result is ``inspired, clever, and unexpected fiction,'' Chris Bohjalian wrote here last year. The men in the stories in WANTING ONLY TO BE HEARD, by Jack Driscoll (University of Massachusetts, $12.95), are even more desperate. Frequently hunters and fishermen, often bereft of female company, they live close to nature _ and to violence. In 1993, Jonis Agee said in the Book Review that ``Jack Driscoll's poetic language gives them a stature that makes their struggle meaningful, tragic and universal.''  MOTHERLESS DAUGHTERS: The Legacy of Loss  By Hope Edelman. Delta, $12.95.  The author, who was 17 when her mother died, draws on clinical research and the experiences of more than 200 other women to analyze the impact of such a loss. Daughters in mourning, she explains, often feel that their own lives are incomplete. This is ``a moving and valuable treatment of a neglected subject,'' Nancy Caldwell Sorel said here last year. Maternal roles are also examined in THE MYTHS OF MOTHERHOOD: How Culture Reinvents the Good Mother, by Shari L. Thurer (Penguin, $12.95). Ranging from the Stone Age to today, this history reveals how society, not biology, has defined women's task as parents. In 1994, our reviewer, Rachel Billington, called it ``positive, sensible and readable.''  ACTS OF LOVE  By Emily Listfield. Penguin, $10.95.  It's a familiar scenario: a gun goes off in a suburban living room, and a marital quarrel ends in death. The question this novel poses is whether Ted Waring deliberately or accidentally killed his estranged wife. The author's ``chilling meditation ... is a sobering reminder that some puzzles are better left unsolved,'' Sarah Ferguson said in the Book Review last year.  THE PROTEAN SELF: Human Resilience in an Age of Fragmentation  By Robert Jay Lifton. Basic Books, $13.  If a foolish consistency is indeed the hobgoblin of little minds, the author has found quite a few large-minded people. Their response to the dislocation of today's world is to become eminently adaptable. In 1994, our reviewer, Richard A. Shweder, found this ``a fascinating and appealing book because it is a near-perfect example of an intellectual stance that skeptics sometimes disparage.'' Human resilience is also evident in THE AGELESS SELF: Sources of Meaning in Late Life, by Sharon R. Kaufman (University of Wisconsin, $11.95). A study of 60 elderly men and women, it demonstrates that strength of spirit and mind often compensates for bodily weakness. It is ``a book that no novelist or indeed any caring person with a fondness for human interiors could resist,'' Paul West said here in 1987.  DROWN ALL THE DOGS  By Thomas Adcock. Pocket Books, $5.50.  Detective Neil Hockaday has to confront his own family's shadowy past when he returns to Ireland to find out about the father he never knew. Last year, our reviewer, Marilyn Stasio, called this mystery's dialogue ``lively and literate.'' </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950508.0111 type=story > <HEADLINE> `FASHION COPS' LURK IN CAPITOL CORRIDORS </HEADLINE> <DATELINE>  (BC-NO-PANTS-CONGRESS-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199505.gz"

(To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a 200-word sidebar By LAURIE TARKAN c.1994 McCall's magazine (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  A home of one's own is part of the American dream, but it can feel like a nightmare if your neighborhood loses its luster _ if, for instance, neighbors make excessive noise, teen-agers loiter late at night or an undesirable business opens nearby.  Luckily, you don't have to suffer many of these difficulties in silence. Here's what you can do to solve some common neighborhood problems. HOW TO TAKE ON TROUBLESOME NEIGHBORS  Typical complaints against neighbors include noise (attributable to everything from a loud stereo to a barking dog to marital spats), property defacement (by children or pets), trees (a neighbor's tree drops crab apples onto your property), fences (those that are too high block your view or are placed on your property) and eyesores (a yard overgrown with weeds).  But the law is usually in your corner. ``Most people are surprised to learn that anything that really disturbs their home is probably against the local law,'' says attorney Mark Warda, author of ``Neighbor vs. Neighbor'' (Sphinx Publishing, 1991). The majority of towns _ from rural outposts to big cities _ have ordinances specifically preventing annoyances.  Before you explore this route, though, try talking _ nicely. Minor problems can quickly escalate into major ones if they aren't handled with diplomacy. The best approach is to talk to your neighbor in person soon after the conflict arises.  If the problem persists, go to your local library to check which law specifically pertains to your neighbor's actions. In most cases, he probably doesn't know his behavior is a violation and presenting him with a copy of the ordinance will get him to stop.  If this doesn't work, consult a mediator _ an objective third party who is trained in helping people come to mutually agreeable solutions and will contact the neighbor. Mediation proves successful 90 percent of the time. Many counties even offer free dispute-resolution services. WAYS TO KEEP TEENS FROM TERRORIZING YOUR COMMUNITY  Vandalism is almost a rite of passage for some kids. Teens' antics can be as harmless as throwing an egg at a house or as destructive as spraying graffiti on walls.  In many communities, citizens are dealing with the problem head-on. ``When it comes to preventing youth crimes, it's not the police but the neighbors who can really have the most impact,'' says Stephanie Mann, co-author of ``Safe Homes, Safe Neighborhoods'' (Nolo Press, 1993), which details how to organize a neighborhood crime-prevention group.  ``When neighbors get organized, it sends a clear message to kids that they care about the neighborhood and that vandalism and crime won't be tolerated.'' Experts agree that it's definitely tougher to commit crimes where neighbors are unified than where they keep to themselves.  The first step in forming a crime-prevention group is to talk with neighbors. A start-up tactics Mann recommends: Conduct a face-to-face survey of your neighbors that asks about their concerns. Then invite them over for coffee to begin a dialogue about the issues.  As a group, she explains, you can organize cleanups of parks or abandoned lots and encourage neighbors to take good care of their property. (A well-maintained neighborhood is less likely to be attacked than one that appears run-down.)  If an abandoned lot is attracting drug dealers or garbage dumpers, the group can ask city officials to cite the owner for violating blighted-property laws, which prohibit property owners from allowing their land to fall into disrepair.  A neighborhood-watch program can also curb teen crime. These encourage residents to improve home security and report any suspicious activities they observe to the police. AN EFFECTIVE TACTIC FOR BLOCKING UNWELCOME BUSINESSES  You hear that a topless bar is going to open near your house or a homeless shelter, garbage dump or some other facility you believe may threaten your quality of life. Don't start packing.  In thousands of communities, NIMBYs _ which stands for ``not in my back yard'' and refers to groups of residents who fight a neighborhood change they perceive as undesirable _ try to block such developments. ``Overall, NIMBY groups are very successful,'' says Norman Krumholz, a professor of urban planning at Cleveland State University.  To block an unwanted building or use of land, residents typically organize, pass out petitions, appear at city- or town-council meetings and lobby local politicians.  Their most effective tactic is to present evidence that supports their concerns. For example, they might call upon real-estate experts who predict a decline in property values or use statistics to show that the presence of certain businesses increases crime. -0- SIDEBAR: NEGOTIATE LIKE A PRO  The way you discuss a conflict with a neighbor can make all the difference between resolving the dispute and making it worse. According to Jon Weiss, executive director of the Center for Conflict Resolution in Chicago, the following simple tactics will help ensure success:  _ FOCUS ON THE PROBLEM, NOT THE INDIVIDUAL. Try to separate the other person's personality or appearance _ which you may have a negative reaction to _ from the dispute at hand. You'll keep the argument from foolishly veering off into counterproductive personal attacks and will be able to channel all your energies into coming to an understanding.  _ BE RECEPTIVE TO SEVERAL SOLUTIONS. Don't get locked into just one way of solving the problem. The more options there are on the table, the sooner you'll reach an agreement. Be creative and present several solutions. Ask the other person for his or her ideas as well.  _ STICK TO YOUR INTERESTS AND NEEDS RATHER THAN YOUR PRINCIPLES. Instead of saying ``No one should play a radio loudly late at night,'' say ``I have trouble falling asleep at night because your music is loud.'' This prevents the other person from feeling judged and will help keep your conversation on track as to the specific nature of the problem.  (Laurie Tarkan is a New York City-based free-lance writer.) ------------------------------------------------- (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950522.0345 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-CRAVING-CURBERS-PAGEUP-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> FOOD THAT CURB YOU CRAVINGS (This ``separate buy'' article is from this week's PageUp Lifestyle and Entertainment Package.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) By LEE RANDALL c.1994 Family Circle magazine (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  When you can't concentrate on your work, get to sleep or find a moment's peace until you've eaten a piece of chocolate (or some cookies, corn chips or ice cream), that's a craving!  Cravings compel people to go to extravagant lengths for satisfaction _ and carry with them the danger of bingeing and piling on unwanted pounds.  But don't despair: There are sane, sensible strategies for keeping these yearnings at bay. WHY WE CRAVE  Do longings for certain foods stem from our physiology or psychology? There are lots of theories, none yet proven. But one thing experts know for certain is that cravings are not your body's way of making you eat what it needs.  ``There is no evidence that people crave specific foods because of a nutritional deficiency,'' says Adam Drewnowski, director of the human nutrition program at the University of Michigan. ``For example, we don't crave chocolate, which is high in magnesium, because we're low in that mineral.''  But these quirky desires for particular foods may be triggered by other physiological occurrences, according to Drewnowski. ``If you crave chocolate, you may really need its relaxing effect on the body,'' he says. ``Chocolate causes endorphins _ the body's natural painkillers _ to be released in the brain.'' High-fat/high-sugar foods, such as chocolate and ice cream, seem to do this most effectively.  Researcher Sarah F. Leibowitz, a neurobiologist at Rockefeller University in New York City, says that higher levels of certain brain chemicals may confer a ``fat tooth,'' enhancing the appetite for high-fat foods. If this theory is true, scientists might one day be able to develop medications to help control this hunger.  Cravings may also be the result of an inborn reaction to stress. The ``fight or flight'' response makes us desire carbohydrates to replenish energy reserves _ needed when we lived in the wild and faced harsh physical challenges.  But nowadays most stress is psychological _ the pressures of work, family and relationships. We need to find better stress-fighters than eating. DIETING WHETS THE APPETITE  Non-stop dieting may seem like a good way to control your weight, but it turns out that the constant denial actually leaves you more vulnerable to temptation.  David Levitsky, professor of nutrition and psychology at Cornell University in Ithaca, N.Y., says, ``Cravings arise primarily from overly restricted eating.''  Other experts believe that prolonged dieting can also have profound effects on the body, leaving you short on endorphins and less able to handle stress and pain _ so you can't relax.  But does that mean you should wave goodbye to your low-fat eating plan? Not if it's providing you with enough calories, nutrients and carbohydrates, Levitsky says. Eating a balanced diet seems to minimize cravings. HANDLING HANKERING  If you understand your temptations, you can beat them. The first step: Keep a journal of your cravings, when they occur and why. Soon you'll see a pattern showing vulnerable times of day and emotional situations that spark the urge to eat.  It's vital to distinguish between ``the general urge to eat vs. the desperate desire for the taste of lemon meringue pie,'' says Cathy Nonas, administrator of the Theodore B. Vanltallie Center for Nutrition and Weight Management of St. Luke's-Roosevelt Hospital in New York City. ``The first impulse could probably be counteracted by a broad range of foods.''  At times when you want something sweet, for example, but no one food comes to mind, try marshmallows, which are sugary but low in fat. Dying for a luscious texture? Try frozen non-fat yogurt.  On the other hand, if your food journal tells you that stress revs up your appetite, try finding non-dietary ways of managing it. Get more exercise, practice yoga, indulge in a massage, seek therapy _ anything that will help alleviate anxiety.  But at those times when nothing but the food you desire will do, experts suggest you eat it. ``Consider making dietary trade-offs and plan for your favorite treats,'' says Bettye Nowlin, who speaks on behalf of the American Dietetic Association.  ``If you really want cold noodles with sesame sauce, that's what you have to have,'' Nonas says. ``Eat the best-tasting version you can find.'' But if you kind of want Chinese food _ not anything specific _ try steamed broccoli with sauce on the side.  In the meantime, don't wait for a magic pill to come along and eliminate your cravings. Take control of them by figuring out what you really love to eat and when you simply must have some. Soon, you'll be calling the shots, instead of letting the food call you.  (Lee Randall is a Hoboken, N.J.-based free-lance writer.) ------------------------------------------------- (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of a scene from ``Tank Girl.'' By CINDY PEARLMAN Distributed by New York Times Special Features  On May 26 the movie ``Casper,'' featuring everyone's favorite friendly ghost, will open in theaters across America.  Getting the comic-book character ready for this big-screen outing wasn't easy. It took $50 million, two years of special-effects planning, 28 trillion computer bytes and, for executive producer Jeffrey Montgomery, a good sense of humor.  That sense of humor came in handy on the days when the biggest and most mind-boggling question on the set was: Why does Casper have only four fingers?  ``It's easier to draw four fingers,'' explains Montgomery, who is also chairman and CEO of The Harvey Entertainment Co., which publishes the Casper comic books.  Montgomery was the one who sold Steven Spielberg and Amblin Entertainment on the idea that the world needed a new Friendly Ghost movie. But he never imagined how difficult such a film would be to make.  After all the work involved in bringing Casper to the big screen, would Montgomery do it again?  Absolutely. In fact, two of his company's other comic-book characters, Baby Huey and Wendy the Witch, are the stars of films that are currently in development.  And they aren't the only ones. One of the hottest concepts in Hollywood these days involves lifting comic-book characters off the drawing board and splashing them across the silver screen.  ``This is the fashionable trend of the moment,'' says Joe Yanerillo, managing editor of Wizard: The Guide to Comics, a monthly magazine that covers the industry.  ```Batman' and `The Mask' woke everyone up (and showed) that these movies could be huge. You have built-in storylines and exciting plots. Comics are natural movies. And this is just the beginning.''  ``The Mask'' (1994) grossed $120 million and the original ``Batman'' (1989) hauled in $251 million. No wonder dozens of comic-book artists are suddenly taking meeting after meeting in La-La land.  Some recent examples of comic book-based films include ``The Crow'' (1994), ``Richie Rich'' (1994) and ``Tank Girl'' (1995).  What's the thinking behind the trend?  ``Baby boomers long for these movies,'' Montgomery says. ``And the past comic movies have surprised people. They haven't been cheesy but entertaining and high-concept.''  Another reason for the glut of such films: recent advances in special-effects technology.  Jim Carrey, who will soon start filming ``The Mask II,'' saw the incredible potential while making the original film.  ``I feel like a kid in a candy store,'' he said at the time. ``I'm at ILM (Industrial Light &AMP; Magic) watching them morph my body into anything. There are no limits to what you can do.''  Hollywood seems to like comic book-based movies because they usually don't require major brain power.  ``There's a hero and there's a villian,'' Carrey said. ``Comic books are also very visual. The stories are high-concept and understood quickly.''  And the books have loyal readers _ many of whom can't wait to see their old friends come to life on the big screen.  Chris O'Donnell, who plays Robin in the forthcoming ``Batman Forever,'' knows the feeling.  ``I used to run home from school to watch `Batman' (on television),'' he says. ``Now I've got the keys to the Batmobile. It's like my childhood dream come true.''  Lori Petty, who plays Tank Girl in the film of the same name, has been in love with comic-book characters for a long time.  ``I remember stashing away all these comics,'' she says. ``As a kid, you'd read them and then play Superhero Queen of the Universe. As Tank Girl, I'm just living this out on a higher budget.''  Of course there's nothing new about comic-book films.  In the '40s and '50s, films and serials such as the ``Flash Gordon'' series, ``The Adventures of Captain Marvel,'' ``Captain America'' and ``Superman'' kept kids entertained at Saturday matinees.  But those films were usually made on the cheap. The special effects were laughable and the stars were B-list.  Now that's changed. Thanks to high-tech special effects, budgets for comic book-based films can reach $40 million or more.  Comic-book artist Rob Liefeld, 27, recently optioned the rights to his ``Prophet'' character to TriStar Films for a reported six figures.  ``Prophet is on par with Batman. His sales are comparable,'' Liefeld says. ``But he's more like a science-fiction superhero than a classic caped crusader.''  Liefeld says he is now meeting with studio executives about his other comic-book creations, including ``Youngblood,'' ``Supreme,'' ``Brigade,'' ``Trool'' and ``Newman.''  Of course, there's no guarantee of success with such films. The comic-book based flops ``Howard the Duck'' (1986) and ``Meteor Man'' (1993) are proof of that.  But a hit can be especially sweet for many reasons _ including merchandising opportunities. Think about Casper notepads. X-Men underwear. Tank Girl toys.  ``Millions of dollars are on the line,'' says Pamela Rutt, vice president of public relations for Marvel Comics, the company that owns the rights to ``Spider-Man,'' ``The X-Men'' and several other popular characters who will soon be coming to the silver screen.  Here's a rundown of some of the comic-book films you can expect to see in the near future:  _ ``Casper'' (opening May 26) is sort of a teen romance with the friendly ghost falling for ``The Addams Family's'' Christina Ricci. The film includes state-of-the-art special effects from Industrial Light &AMP; Magic.  _ ``Batman Forever'' (June 16) is the third in the modern movie series about the Caped Crusader. This time Val Kilmer steps into the rubber costume. He teams with the Boy Wonder (O'Donnell) and meets new Gotham girl Nicole Kidman. Their mission? To foil the plans of the Riddler (Jim Carrey in an orange wig) and Harvey Two Face (Tommy Lee Jones).  _ ``Judge Dredd'' (June 30) stars Sylvester Stallone in the title role as a futuristic superhero. It is based on a British comic-book series.  _ ``The Fantastic Four'' is a $40 million-plus film in development. Chris Columbus (``Home Alone,'' ``Mrs. Doubtfire'') will reportedly direct a story based on the Marvel Comic superheroes: Mr. Fantastic, the Invisible Girl, the Human Torch and the Thing.  _ ``Spider-man'' will have you climbing the walls soon enough. The lavish, big-budget, special-effects bonanza should start filming next year under the direction of James (``Terminator'') Cameron. Arnold Schwarzenegger has been mentioned as a possibility for the role of the villain.  _ ``Godzilla'' will be directed by ``Speed's'' Jan DeBont. Filming is expected to begin later this year.  _ ``Prophet,'' about a regular guy-turned-superhero, should hit theaters next year. The story revolves around a man who volunteers for a series of experiments and is transformed into a supersoldier.  _ A new ``Superman'' film is reportedly in the works. Producer Jon Peters is talking about finding a younger Superman (sorry, Christopher Reeve). Casting is still up in the air, so to speak.  _ ``The Incredible Hulk'' is reportedly being developed by action producer Gale Ann Hurd. No word on casting as of yet.  Other A-list projects in the works include: ``X-Men,'' produced by ``Lethal Weapon'' director Richard Donner; ``Dr. Octopus,'' based on the Marvel comic series; ``Luke Cage'' or ``Cage,'' also based on a Marvel comic; and ``Blade,'' which director Ernest Dickerson is developing. c.1995 Cindy Pearlman  (Cindy Pearlman is a Chicago-based free-lance writer.) ------------------------------------------------- (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950522.0347 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-MEL-GIBSON-BRAVEHEART-PAGEUP-ART-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> MEL GIBSON PUTS HIS `HEART' AND SOUL INTO PERIOD PIECE (This ``separate buy'' article is from this week's PageUp Lifestyle and Entertainment Package.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of Mel Gibson in a scene from ``Braveheart.'' By IAN SPELLING Distributed by New York Times Special Features  Mel Gibson never considered himself a connoisseur of epic films. Nor was he ever much of an expert on Scottish history.  Yet when the script for ``Braveheart'' _ a three-hour saga about the 13th-century Scottish hero William Wallace _ came to the actor's attention late in 1993, he couldn't resist it. Gibson knew right away that he wanted not only to star in the film but also to produce and direct it.  ``It's a great story. It had everything: love, honor, conflict, life and death and all of the stuff,'' Gibson says during an interview at a Los Angeles hotel.  ``I liked the scope and the range of where it took me. I'd already shot it in my head three times before I said I wanted to direct it.''  Executives at Paramount Pictures, the studio that financed ``Braveheart,'' knew from the start how excited Gibson was about the script.  ``Which is always a bad thing,'' Gibson says, ``because they tend to take advantage of that.''  Yet he ended up having a ``really good experience'' with the studio.  ``They were very supportive of me and gave me everything I needed,'' he says.  And he needed a lot. Bringing ``Braveheart'' to the screen meant lavishing well over $50 million on location shooting in Scotland and Ireland, hiring up to 2,000 extras and hundreds of trained horses for some scenes, designing thousands of detailed period costumes and constructing numerous elaborate sets.  There was also the matter of staging two extended, brutal battle sequences between Wallace's ragtag band of Scottish freedom fighters and the more polished, heavily armed forces of the British king, Edward the Longshanks (Patrick McGoohan).  Before beginning production, Gibson pored through history books to learn as much as he could about Sir William Wallace, a figure whose exploits have been steeped in a combination of fact and legend.  He then set out to assemble a cast that, while relatively low on star power, possessed the acting chops to pull off their respective roles.  Among those actors are McGoohan, best known to American television audiences as ``The Prisoner'' in the classic 1960s series of the same name; French actress Sophie Marceau as Longshank's reluctant daughter-in-law, Princess Isabella; newcomer Angus McFadyen as Robert the Bruce, a would-be king of Scotland and supposed Wallace ally; and Catherine McCormack as Wallace's beloved wife, whose murder at the hands of the British compels him to drive the English out of Scotland once and for all.  ``All of these people are wonderful,'' Gibson says. ``I just looked for the best actresses and actors I could find.  ``I was quite fortunate to come up with people who looked as they should and who could behave like the characters.''  The actors apparently felt just as fortunate to be working on a project headed by Gibson.  McCormack says she was pleasantly surprised to find that the 39-year-old star of the ``Lethal Weapon'' and ``Mad Max'' trilogies wasn't nearly as intimidating as she imagined.  ``He made it all very comfortable,'' she explains during a separate interview. ``He's very down to earth, no ego.  ``Most of my scenes were with him, so there was very little direction involved. We sort of just did the scenes, improvised a bit, and if things didn't work we talked it through.  ``It was a great experience working with him. I think he's directed an amazing film.''  Gibson, who made his directing debut with the respectable but imperfect film drama ``The Man Without a Face'' (1993), says that at no time did he feel as if he'd bitten off more than he could chew with ``Braveheart.''  That confidence probably came from the endless preparation he did _ everything from getting himself into ideal physical shape to storyboarding the immense battle sequences in detail.  ``I didn't go in and just do it off the top of my head,'' Gibson says. ``I went in armed to the teeth.  ``I ate my Wheaties, took vitamins, went running, brought a chiropractor along and was as clearheaded as I could possibly be. That was just on a personal level.  ``The everyday work situations also called for planning. Those battle sequences were done first on a table for three weeks with plastic soldiers to make them as cinematic as possible and so that I could organize the strategy.  ``Then it took six weeks just to shoot one battle, because I wanted the audience to feel as if they were really in the middle of those battles.''  When the film is released May 24 it will begin its own battle _ for box-office receipts. Because it comes on the heels of ``Rob Roy'' (the Scottish period piece starring Liam Neeson) and because it has a three-hour running time (which will limit the number of times a theater can show the picture in a given day), ``Braveheart'' could have a hard time attracting large audiences and recouping its production costs.  Gibson, however, quickly dismisses both concerns.  ```Rob Roy' is a totally different film,'' he says earnestly. ``It's a little story, like a Western.  ``It doesn't explore the same territory at all, and it's set in a different time frame.''  And the film's length?  ``I knew `Braveheart' would be longish, and I signed a contract that said I could come in at two hours and 15 minutes. I showed the studio the rough cut, which was three hours and 45 minutes long, and they threw out the contract.  ``They said, `That's OK, just cut some out.' So I cut out an hour. I think we'll do fine.''  He's equally confident about his latest acting project: an animated version of ``Pocahantas'' that the Walt Disney Co. is hoping will be its next blockbuster along the lines of ``The Lion King'' and ``Beauty and the Beast.''  Gibson _ whose last film, the 1994 comedy-Western ``Maverick,'' was a hit both domestically and internationally _ provides the voice of ``Pocahontas''' British captain John Smith. He sheepishly reveals, however, that his big singing number didn't make the film's final cut.  ``I guess they only want me to sing in the shower,'' Gibson jokes. ``The film is good, actually, really well done.  ``Disney always does those animated films pretty well. I did it for my kids and they liked it a lot.''  With two films about to open (``Pocahantas'' hits theaters June 23) and his production company, Icon Productions, at work on several new projects, Gibson has more than a few irons in the fire.  Yet his No. 1 priority for the next few months is something pretty simple.  ``Rest,'' he says, feigning exhaustion. ``Rest. I've got to recharge the old batteries.'' c.1995 Ian Spelling  (Ian Spelling is a New York-based free-lance writer.) GREETINGS FROM MINNESOBER (2,000 words) By STEVEN LEVY  Thanks to the Hazelden Foundation rehab clinic, Minnesota has long been considered a great place to drop an addiction. It's where the 12 steps of Alcoholics Anonymous are worshiped as fervently as the 10 Commandments, where ``drunks'' and ``junkies'' were first renamed ``chemically dependent.'' But more recently, the state has emerged as a mecca for spiritual transformation of every stripe. Neal Karlen, a Minneapolis-based writer, offers a tongue-in-cheek feature on why this state of 4.5 million located in the middle of Flyoverland is known as the sanitarium state. Nicholas Wade, a regular contributor to The New York Times Magazine, reports that earth is not immune to celestial bombardment and may not be a very safe place. By EARTHA KITT c.1995 The Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists  As part of Women in the Arts Speak Out, a monthly series, Eartha Kitt tells how she was blacklisted during the Vietnam War after rocking the boat at a luncheon with Lady Bird Johnson. (slugged: BC-EARTHA-KITT-REBUKES-McNAMARA-NYTSF, priority code r, category codes k,i)  NOTE: Next month in this series, Bianca Jagger, filmmaker and model, speaks out about global domestic violence. LIFE IN HOLLYWOOD'S PAST LANE By PAUL BETTS c.1995 The Financial Times  Los Angeles-based Grave Line Tours has offered tours of the darker side of Hollywood for the past eight years. Business is booming. High points include the bungalow where John Belushi died, the home where Bugsy Siegel was shot, infamous sites in the O.J. Simpson case, the grave of Douglas Fairbanks Sr., and the restaurant where James Dean had his last supper. (slugged: BC-HOLLYWOOD-GRAVES-NYTSF, priority code r, category codes e,t)  MIRANDA RICHARDSON: THE RELUCTANT STAR By LOUISE FARR c.1995 Fairchild Publications  Moviegoers were captivated by Miranda Richardson's portrayal of Vivian Haigh-Wood, T.S. Eliot's wife and muse, in the movie ``Tom &AMP; Viv'' (1994), for which Richardson earned a Best Actress Oscar nomination. But despite all the Hollywood clamor following the Academy Awards, the 26-year-old English actress still plays the role of reluctant star far better. Instead, she lets her characters do the talking. Merle Ginsberg, entertainment editor for Fairchild Publications, interviews Richardson, who talks the small number of good roles for actresses in America, and consequently why she has worked mostly in British films. (slugged: BC-MIRANDA-RICHARDSON-INTERVIEW-NYTSF, priority code r, category code e)  HOLLYWOOD'S BILLION-DOLLAR `SUMMER OF EXCESS' World rights except Boston, Great Britain and Australia. Color slides of actor Sylvester Stallone from ``Judge Dredd'' and actors Sean Connery and Richard Gere from ``First Knight'' available. B&AMP;W photos of actor Kevin Costner from ``Waterworld'' and actor Mel Gibson from ``Braveheart'' also available. By IVOR DAVIS Distributed by New York Times Special Features  Hollywood has always been known as an empire of excess. But this year, as the summer movie blockbuster season approaches, studios are spending no less than $1 billion dollars to keep us entertained. How did this happen? Hollywood-based journalist Ivor Davis investigates. His report includes an interview with John Davis, one of the producers of Kevin Costner's upcoming film ``Waterworld'' _ at $180 million, the most expensive movie of all time. 1,100 words. (slugged: BC-IVOR-BILLION-DOLLAR-MOVIES-ART-NYTSF, priority code r, category code e)  LIFESTYLE:  CHARITY FOR THE RICH By JOE ROGALY c.1995 The Financial Times  Rich families _ those with total net assets of $20 million or more _ often fail to transfer their wealth to their families. Often they make charitable contributions to avoid greedy children and evade taxes. And ordinary wage earners seem to give more to charity than millionaires. Joe Rogaly is a business correspondent for The Financial Times of London. 900 words. (slugged: BC-MILLIONAIRES-STINGY-NYTSF, priority code r, category codes l,i)  WHEN DAD WAS SOBER, HE WAS A GOOD FATHER By ANDREA TODD c.1995 The New York Times Magazine  When her father was drunk, he beat her mother and terrorized the family. When he wasn't, he coached his daughters in swimming and took them to see fire trucks and spent weeks searching for their lost dog. In a gripping and poignant tribute, California-based writer Andrea Todd explains why, despite the pain, she still has faith in her father. 1,250 words. (slugged: BC-ABUSIVE-DAD-REPENTS-ABT.MEN/HERS-NYTSF, priority code r, category code l)  THE NEWT SOCIETY By SUSAN WATTERS c.1995 Fairchild Publications  No more Georgetown dinner parties, liberal salons and elegant elitists who used to dominate Washington. To conservative Republicans, reforming Washington's social scene is as essential as dismantling the welfare state. These days, House Speaker Newt Gingrich and his cohorts are bypassing the capital's old social order and setting up their own salons, their own hostesses, their own think tanks and their own media outlets. Susan Watters, the Washington Bureau Director for Fairchild Publications, reports on how conservative culture is conquering the capital. (slugged: BC-D.C.'S-NEW-SOCIAL-SCENE-NYTSF, priority code r, category codes l and a)  CENTERPIECE:  Greetings from Minnesober  Her Own Private Tailhook  Duck! The Sky is Falling  Horseless in Cyberspace, by Max Frankel  The above articles are Centerpiece selections from The New York Times Magazine for release Sun., May 28. They are for release as one-shots Sun., May 28.  GREETINGS FROM MINNESOBER (2,000 words) By STEVEN LEVY  Thanks to the Hazelden Foundation rehab clinic, Minnesota has long been considered a great place to drop an addiction. It's where the 12 steps of Alcoholics Anonymous are worshiped as fervently as the 10 Commandments, where ``drunks'' and ``junkies'' were first renamed ``chemically dependent.'' But more recently, the state has emerged as a mecca for spiritual transformation of every stripe. Neal Karlen, a Minneapolis-based writer, offers a tongue-in-cheek feature on why this state of 4.5 million located in the middle of Flyoverland is known as the sanitarium state. (slugged: BC-MINNESOTA-REHAB-STATE-$adv28-NYTSF, priority code s, category code c)  HER OWN PRIVATE TAILHOOK (2,500 words) FROM THE NEW YORK TIMES MAGAZINE By LAURA PALMER Distributed by New York Times Special Features  Prisoner 95, 19-year-old Libby Saum, was deprived of food and sleep. She was physically and sexually abused by her captors. This did not happen in Baghdad or Bosnia, but at the U.S. Air Force Academy in Colorado. It was all part of a sexual-exploitation survival course that, according to Saum, was used by many cadets as an excuse to brutalize their peers. Laura Palmer is the author of ``Shrapnel in the Heart: Letters and Remembrances from the Vietnam Veterans Memorial,'' Random House, 1987. (slugged:BC-SEXUALLY-HARASSED-CADET-CENTERPIECE-$adv28-NYTSF, priority code s, category code c)  DUCK! THE SKY IS FALLING (1,200 words) By NICHOLAS WADE  Tell someone that planet Earth needs a defense system against asteroids and they may laugh a little nervously. But raise the question with astronomers or planetary geologists and they will all agree. According to geological catastrophism, periodic mass extinctions of species, such as the dinosaurs 65 million years, may have been caused by the impact of asteroids crashing into the earth. Nicholas Wade, a regular contributor to The New York Times Magazine, reports that earth is not immune to celestial bombardment and may not be a very safe place. (slugged: BC-ASTEROID-DEFENSE-CENTERPIECE-$adv28-NYTSF, priority code s, category code c)  HORSELESS IN CYBERSPACE (1,100 words) By MAX FRANKEL  Skeptics insist that technology has had a terrible effect on communication _ offering pleasure at the price of spiritual devastation. Yet journalists are obsessed with the computer. The advances may be like a horseless carriage that is full of benefits but also dreadfully pollutes the environment. Max Frankel is a Pulitzer Prize-winning journalist and former executive editor of The New York Times. (slugged: BC-FRANKEL-CENTERPIECE-BONUS-$adv28-NYTSF, priority code s, category code c)  Thank you very much,  The New York Times Syndicate  (New York headquarters: 212-499-3300 or 1-800-972-3550; fax, 212-499-3382.) </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950522.0351 type=advis > <DATELINE>  (BC-PREMIUM-ADVISORY-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> U.S. Marine Bugler Befriends Japanese Dive Bomber Who sunk his Ship  Preventing a Mideast War _ Rather than Fighting One  Eartha Kitt: On Being Blacklisted During the Vietnam War and Robert S. NEWS &AMP; COMMENTARY:  U.S. MARINE BUGLER BEFRIENDS JAPANESE DIVE BOMBER WHO SUNK HIS SHIP By RICHARD HALLORAN  ``I was a bugler aboard the West Virginia,'' said Richard Fiske. ``That was my target,'' replied Zenji Abe. The two men met by accident last year in Honolulu. Since then they have become friends. Abe gave Fiske $300 to buy roses. When Fiske visits Pearl Harbor, he plays taps and drops two roses in the water. A behind-the-scenes story by Richard Halloran, former military correspondent for The New York Times. 1,000 words. WASHINGTON NEWS (``w'' category)  COURT-TERM-LIMITS (Washington) _ In a major blow to supporters of congressional term limits, the Supreme Court rules, 5-4, that states may not place restrictions on how long members of the House and Senate may serve in Washington. By DAN FREEDMAN.  U.S.EMBASSY-ISRAEL (Washington) _ Arab-American organizations and some religious groups attack Senate Majority Leader Bob Dole's proposal to relocate the U.S. embassy in Israel from Tel Aviv to Jerusalem. They say such a move could jeopardize the Middle East peace process. By COLIN GREY.  BARBOUR-GRAMM (Washington) _ Republican national chairman Haley Barbour says Sen. Phil Gramm's 1974 investment in a porn movie will have no impact on his candidacy for the GOP presidential nomination. By ELIZABETH SCHWINN.  INTERNATIONAL NEWS (``i'' category)  U.S.-FRANCE (Paris) _ U.S.-French relations, always tricky and now at a low point, may improve under the new French president, Jacques Chirac. Diplomats note that during his 35 years in politics, Chirac has avoided anti-American posturing common to many French officials. By BERNARD D. KAPLAN.  COMMENTARY (``k'' category)  MEANS-COLUMN (Washington) _ An unexpected victim of conspiracy-minded, anti-government militants is a governors' conference meant to discuss future federal-state relationships. Its sponsors have had to go back to the drawing board. WASHINGTON NEWS (``w'' category)  COURT-TERM-LIMITS (Washington) _ In a major blow to supporters of congressional term limits, the Supreme Court rules, 5-4, that states may not place restrictions on how long members of the House and Senate may serve in Washington. By DAN FREEDMAN.  U.S.EMBASSY-ISRAEL (Washington) _ Arab-American organizations and some religious groups attack Senate Majority Leader Bob Dole's proposal to relocate the U.S. embassy in Israel from Tel Aviv to Jerusalem. They say such a move could jeopardize the Middle East peace process. By COLIN GREY.  BARBOUR-GRAMM (Washington) _ Republican national chairman Haley Barbour says Sen. Phil Gramm's 1974 investment in a porn movie will have no impact on his candidacy for the GOP presidential nomination. By ELIZABETH SCHWINN.  INTERNATIONAL NEWS (``i'' category)  U.S.-FRANCE (Paris) _ U.S.-French relations, always tricky and now at a low point, may improve under the new French president, Jacques Chirac. Diplomats note that during his 35 years in politics, Chirac has avoided anti-American posturing common to many French officials. By BERNARD D. KAPLAN.  COMMENTARY (``k'' category)  MEANS-COLUMN (Washington) _ An unexpected victim of conspiracy-minded, anti-government militants is a governors' conference meant to discuss future federal-state relationships. Its sponsors have had to go back to the drawing board. Bowles is almost cheerful when he admits to emotional remoteness, a consequence, he explains, of parents who showed him no love after he was born in Jamaica, N.Y., in 1910. Composer Ned Rorem provides the major assessment of Bowles' other creative body of work _ his music. Spare, compressed, elegant and often jaunty in the manner of Satie and Les Six, his compositions include chamber and vocal works and music for stage productions by Orson Welles, Lillian Hellman and Tennessee Williams. Times News Service </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950614.0561 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-LEAH-PERSONALS-SFCHRON) </DATELINE> <TEXT> (Eds: This story also moved in the ``a'' national news file) By LEAH GARCHIK c.1995 San Francisco Chronicle  SPREAD THE WORD: MIRACLE RECOVERY  Late last month at the San Francisco Zoo, 11 Tibetan monks came a-chanting.  When the monks, from Gyuto Tantric University in India, showed up for a behind-the-scenes tour of the facilities, they told zoo directors that their chants had certain healing powers. Were there any unhealthy animals in the house?  There was, in fact: Shin the snow leopard, wasting away from digestive problems that had ruined her appetite.  The media were called and the monks assembled. The snow leopard was up in a tree, looking depressed. But once the chanting began, the story goes, Shin climbed down 15 feet, sat just in front of the monks and seemed quite captivated by the chanting. When the show was over, she walked away in what seemed to be better spirits. And later that day, Shin actually had some food.  Ann Fraser of KPIX did a full report for Bay Area TV viewers, and an Associated Press story went out across the nation: The monks had saved the snow leopard.  This was just the kind of news _ miracle wrought by holy men _ that appealed to the Religious News Service, which weighed in with its own version of events. The Voice of America did a story, too, and then National Public Radio, whose five-minute piece was broadcast this week. Whereupon, said zoo public relations director Nancy Chan, NBC News called, and then the Sunday Times of London.  Unfortunately, the miracle patient had passed on to that big jungle in the sky last Friday.  ``I just got a call from a reporter in Boca Raton, Fla.,'' Chan said. ``I told her that the snow leopard had died. She said maybe she wouldn't do the story.''  BARBIE GOES ALL, LIKE, SPORTY  The Atlanta Committee for the Olympic Games has licensed an Olympic edition of the Barbie doll. ``Barbie is always doing whatever people, kids, are interested in,'' said Mattel spokeswoman Lisa McKendall.  The $19.99 plastic figurine series will include Barbies dressed in Olympic uniforms (outfits, in Barbie lingo) of various countries.  GORILLAS IN THE SMOKE?  The Rubbish Rangers of Leal Elementary School in Mission, Texas, have sent word of their latest campaign: By Earth Day, April 22, 1996, they want to have smoking abolished in zoos all over the world.  The Rangers have sent letters to zoos all across the country: ``Smoking pollutes the air and can be hazardous to the animals' and visitors' health. ... Animals should be protected from smoke in their zoo environment.''  At the San Francisco Zoo, smoking is prohibited in all buildings, but allowed in outdoor areas. Officials said that zoo rules are rules that prevail at all San Francisco facilities.  If the zebras want protection from secondhand smoke, said the zoo folk, they'll have to prevail on city bureaucrats.  APES FIRST  Analysts at Inside Media note with surprise this week that advertising for the new movie ``Congo'' includes credits for writer, director, producer, score and editor, but none at all for the actors.  The movie stars Laura Linney and Dylan Walsh, neither of them well-known, and Tim Curry and Ernie Hudson, who are better known.  A source ``close to the film,'' which was based on a novel by Michael Crichton, explained:  ``The focal point for the publicity is that `Congo' is by the author of `Jurassic Park.' And they wanted to emphasize the gorillas.''  HOW TO RAISE MONEY IN THESE GENEROUS TIMES  Michael Musto of the Village Voice describes a recent fund-raiser at a brand-new Manhattan theme restaurant called the Fashion Cafe. The event was organized by DISHES, an acronym for Determined Involved Supermodels Helping to End Suffering, he explains, to raise money to fight pediatric AIDS.  It was highlighted by an auction of clothes that were displayed on mannequins whose heads were covered with paper bags on which likenesses of Sharon Stone and James Carville were painted. In the background, writes Musto, was ``a samba band rollicking through `Everything Gives You Cancer.' ''  A good time may have been had by all. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19950614.0562 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-LEAH-PERSONALS-SFCHRON) </DATELINE> <TEXT> (Eds: This story also moved in the ``l'' lifestyle file) By LEAH GARCHIK c.1995 San Francisco Chronicle  SPREAD THE WORD: MIRACLE RECOVERY  Late last month at the San Francisco Zoo, 11 Tibetan monks came a-chanting. "31773"	"he"	"explains"	" One of Walker's current projects involves regilding an immense 19th-century mirror. Gilding, he explains, involves more than merely slapping on some gold leaf. Preparatory layers culminate in ``bole,'' a mix of clay and rabbit-skin glue that comes in colors and gives the gilding depth. To get the gold on, Walker strokes his face with a brush, to create static electricity that will then pick up the ethereal flecks of 23-karat gold, which the sizing in the bole will pull down flat. (1,300) By PERUCCI FERRAIUOLO  In the early 1920s Hollywood discovered that people love being scared _ as long as they are sitting with a few hundred other people experiencing the same thing. With the advent of the horror film came another Hollywood mainstay _ the horror-film star. Perucci Ferraiuolo, a Kirkland, Wash.-based free-lance writer, reports on four of the silver screen's horror greats: Lon Chaney, Boris Karloff, Bela Lugosi and Vincent Price. But myths, legends and lore are not confined to the United States. People the world over believe all kinds of strange notions. In Asia, if you accidentally burn a nightclub hostess with a cigarette it will bring her good luck. Kevin Keating, a Sausalito-based free-lance writer, has compiled a roundup of superstitions worldwide.
