(To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a 250-word sidebar By JOHN KEHOE c.1995 A&AMP;E Monthly magazine (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  I like to cook. I like to read. I love to read cookbooks, joyously, uncritically, for sheer pleasure.  But in the kitchen I demand that a cookbook be well written, well ordered, authoritative, that it not presuppose a level of skill among its users unless explicitly stated and that it be handsomely produced.  And it must be more than merely fashionable. Culinary trends come and go. The cookbooks that live up to these criteria are both valuable tools and things of beauty.  What follows is a personal list of in-print books I think are worth attention. -0-  1. ``The Joy of Cooking'' by Irma Rombauer (North American Library, 1989). The cornerstone of the kitchen library.  Utterly American (containing perhaps the definitive recipe for strawberry shortcake), marked by an odd, sly wit, containing a large number of well-detailed recipes and also giving the beginning or nearly proficient cook what he or she needs most: a sense of achievement, confidence and the will to attempt greater challenges.  As essential in the kitchen as a stove.  2. ``The Fannie Farmer Cookbook'' by Marion Cunningham (Knopf, 1990). The other cornerstone.  Deep, if not wide, accessible, dependable, it offers beginners exactly what ``Joy of Cooking'' does but from a different, equally valuable angle. Some people would say both books aren't necessary. I don't.  3. ``The New Basics'' by Shelia Lukins and Julee Rosso (Workman, 1989). The Lukins-Rosso team, of Silver Palate fame, have produced perhaps the only general, wide-ranging cookbook of the past 30 years that even approaches the depth and ease of use of the above-listed classics. Its more contemporary sensibility might make it a bit more appealing to the modern reader.  4. and 5. ``The New James Beard'' by James Beard (Knopf, 1984) and ``The Theory and Practice of Good Cooking'' by James Beard (Random House, 1990). James Beard was arguably the man who made American cooks take their craft seriously. He wrote more than a dozen books, all of them lively and informative, but these are of particular note.  Distinctive recipes and impeccable organization, but the illuminating principle here is the very evident delight Beard took in cooking and, more important, eating.  6. ``The Chez Panisse Menu Cookbook'' by Alice Waters (Random House, 1982). The recipes are detailed and challenging. For those who don't know their way around the cutting board, there are easier places to start. But a serious cook must have this book. Try the ``Baked Stuffed Nectarines,'' a totally marvellous dessert.  7. and 8. ``Jeremiah Tower's New American Classics'' by Jeremiah Tower (Random House, 1986) and ``The Union Square Cafe Cookbook'' by Danny Meyer and Michael Romano (HarperCollins, 1994). Two completely different esthetics _ Tower representing California cuisine in all its initial innocence and purity, the Union Square team standing in for the sophisticated New York City contingent. Both are remarkably successful.  Tower's ``Cream of Fresh Corn Soup, Mendicino Style'' will take your breath away, while Union Square's ``Hot Garlic Potato Chips'' will bring tears of joy to your eyes.  9. ``Martha Stewart's Menus for Entertaining'' by Martha Stewart (Crown, 1994). The caveat about Stewart's books is that she writes for cooking in a fully equipped gourmet kitchen and for cooks with years of experience and a trained staff. Still, she's very good and this book is her best.  10. ``The Frugal Gourmet Cooks with Wine'' by Jeff Smith (Morrow, 1986). Cooking with wine is underappreciated. The result is great food and Smith does it as well as it can be done. The recipe for ``Beef Bourguignon'' alone is worth the price.  11. ``Mastering the Art of French Cooking,'' Vol. 1 by Julia Child (Knopf, 1979). Child is magnifique. Her directions and prose are as clear as a bell, her technique unrivaled. This book is enough to keep a cook busy for years. And there are two volumes.  12. and 13. ``Pierre Franey's Cooking In America'' by Pierre Franey (Knopf, 1994) and ``Simply French'' by Patricia Wells (Morrow, 1991). These two books are good backups for Child. The ``Creamy Cucumber Salad with Curry'' from ``Simply French'' is casual French cuisine at its best.  14. ``Essentials of Classic Italian Cooking'' by Marcella Hazan (Knopf, 1992). Hazan does for Italy what Child does for France and keeps to the same stratospheric standards. Simple, direct, fabulous recipes _ the single best book on ``high kitchen'' Italian cooking now extant.  15. ``Pasta Classica'' by Julia Della Croce (Chronicle Books, 1987). Like this book, pasta is deceptively simple _ the more you eat it, the more you appreciate it. Casual, breezy, but fully informed and beautifully designed and illustrated _ this is a wonderful title.  16., 17. and 18. ``Tommy Tang's Modern Thai Cuisine'' by Tommy Tang (Doubleday, 1991), ``Ken Hom's Chinese Kitchen'' by Ken Hom (Hyperion, 1995) and ``China Moon Cookbook'' by Barbara Tropp (Workman, 1992).  The subtle complexity of Thai food is explicated in an admirably direct manner by Tommy Tang. Barbara Tropp's educated and appreciative take on Chinese cooking is a recommended introduction, after which Ken Hom's slightly more technical and demanding directions are easier to understand and carry out.  19. and 20. ``The Cuisines of Mexico'' by Diana Kennedy (HarperCollins, 1989) and ``Coyote Cafe Cookbook'' by Mark Miller (Ten Speed Press, 1989). Kennedy writes on authentic Mexican cooking in an imperious, even regal manner. Miller writes about the hybrid Southwestern cuisine in an earnest, almost eager-to-please tone. Two unquestioned masters of their genres.  Miller's crackling-with-excitement creativity is showcased in recipes such as ``Blue Corn Trout.'' Kennedy's voice of tradition and authority leads the initiate through the subtle complexities of a mole sauce. Both, highly recommended.  21. ``The Wolfgang Puck Cookbook'' by Wolfgang Puck (Random House, 1995). Infused with the simple joy of cooking fine food.  22. ``Sauces'' by James Petersen (Van Nostrand Reinhold, 1991). Closer to a textbook than a cookbook, this is the definitive volume on the preparation and creation of any sauce, from bearnaise to ketchup. Well written, informative and extremely interesting.  23. ``In the Kitchen with Rosie'' by Rosie Daley (Knopf, 1994). This isn't just a low-fat, heart-smart cookbook _ it's a publishing phenomena, with millions of copies in print. And the food tastes great!  24. ``Eating Well with Burt Wolf'' by Burt Wolf (Doubleday, 1994). Wolf was cooking light before we knew we had to. This is a well-considered, taste-tested route to healthy eating. His TV show is also fun.  25. ``Sundays at Moosewood'' by the Moosewood Collective (Fireside/Simon and Schuster, 1990). For the ever-increasing vegetarian population, this is an excellent, tried-and-true guide to meatless meals.  26. ``The Thrill of the Grill'' by Chris Schlesinger and John Willoughby (Morrow, 1990). This book only fans the flames of one's passion for cooking over an open fire. It contains explicit directions on the proper care and maintenance of the fire and a slew of absolutely impeccable recipes.  27. ``Around the World'' by Shelia Lukins (Workman, 1995). Every now and then, one is seized by the desire to use exotic ingredients such as toasted sesame seeds or apple chutney. When such a moment strikes, be glad Lukins has traversed the globe and collected a great number of wonderful recipes that employ exactly such ingredients.  28. ``Chef Paul Prudhomme's Louisiana Kitchen'' by Paul Prudhomme (Morrow, 1984). Culinary trends may come and go, but there are exceptions. New Orleans may be the unrecognized culinary capital of America _ and Prudhomme is revered there. The recipes are plainly stated: The book is well organized and the results are memorable in the extreme.  29. ``A Taste of Old Cuba'' by Maria Josefa Lluria de O'Higgins (HarperCollins, 1994). Fascinating as a memoir of a time and place gone forever and equally as enthralling as a cookbook proper, this is an exceptional volume. The recipes _ including those for a totally wonderful ``Garlic Soup'' and a classic, classical ``Flan'' _ are clear, direct and exemplary.  30. ``The Cake Bible'' by Rose Beranbaum (Morrow, 1988). Exactly what it says it is: everything you ever wanted to know about cakes. This is a big, thick, delicious book. It can make one feel full just looking through it.  31. ``Mrs. Fields' I Love Chocolate! Cookbook'' by Debbie Fields (Time-Life, 1994). There are many other chocolate cookbooks. There are other authors perhaps more esteemed than cookie-maven Fields. But this is the book that has the recipe for a ``Double Fudge Chip Cake'' that is heavenly! -0- SIDEBAR: OUT-OF-PRINT, BUT NOT OUT OF MIND  Cookbooks are lately being collected for a variety of reasons. Culinary historians use them as a record of food trends; professional chefs use them as sourcebooks for old dishes to resurrect with a new spin; and book collectors appreciate their rarity and potential profitability. Cookbooks are after all heavily used and easily damaged. A first edition of ``The Joy of Cooking'' in good condition could easily bring thousands of dollars.  I keep an eye out for interesting cookbooks at yard and book sales, depending on whim and circumstances to put into my hands what it may. If I were looking for one title in particular, I could probably find it at one of the bookstores listed below, all of which specialize in ``literature culinaire'' and maintain a stock of new and out-of-print books.  _ Kitchen Arts and Letters, 1435 Lexington Ave., New York, N.Y. 10128; (212) 876-5550.  _ The Cookbook Cottage, 1279 Bardstown Road, Louisville, Ken. 40204; (502) 458-5227.  _ The Cook's Library, 8373 W. 3rd St., Los Angeles, Calif. 90048; (213) 655-3141.  _ Powell's Books for Cooks, 3739 S.E. Hawthorne, Portland, Ore. 97214; (503) 235-3802. (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) By KARYN REPINSKI c.1995 Longevity magazine (Distributed by New York Times Special Features)  Every generation seems to live and die by its own set of beauty myths.  In our grandmothers' era, women counted on the fact that 100 strokes a day would keep their hair shiny and healthy. Similarly, our mothers stocked their cupboards with Jell-O, partly because they believed that consuming gelatin would make their nails grow stronger.  Considering how hungry women are for some hard-and-fast rules about holding onto their looks, who can blame them for buying into these myths?  Indeed, the same could be said of women today. While those old notions may now sound rather foolish, in reality they've been replaced by a whole new crop of beauty myths.  Here are 10 of them and some help separating fact from fiction. -0- MYTH NO. 1: DRINKING LOTS OF WATER KEEPS SKIN YOUTHFUL-LOOKING  Of all the beauty myths, this is the one that most annoys Dr. Seth L. Matarasso, a San Francisco-based dermatologist. ``Women have got to stop drinking 100 glasses of water a day,'' he says.  Acknowledging that water helps clean the kidneys and act as an appetite suppressant, he says that short of bloating you up and temporarily getting rid of some wrinkling, ``it doesn't do a whole lot for the complexion.'' MYTH NO. 2: SOAP IS BAD FOR THE SKIN  ``Women have been bullied: `You use soap on your skin? Oh, my word!''' says Dr. Amy E. Newburger, a Scarsdale, N.Y.-based dermatologist.  Technically a mix of animal fats _ called tallow _ and fruit or vegetable oils _ such as coconut and palm _ traditional soap, which has a high pH, can in fact be drying, particularly to aging skin.  But these days the definition of soap has been expanded to include cleansers formulated with synthetic surfactants, which cleanse skin in a milder manner than true soap.  Moreover, according to David Lozano, vice president of research and development at Physicians Formula, maker of two new Gentle Cleansing Facial Bars, it's possible to add ingredients such as emollients to these bars that are ``benevolent'' to skin. Such soaps are often referred to as ``superfatted.''  If you prefer that ``squeaky-clean'' feeling soap provides, however, there's nothing wrong with using it. One dermatologist says she has some 80-year-old patients with ``gorgeous'' skin who have used it every day of their lives. MYTH NO. 3: SCALP MASSAGE CAN TURN AROUND HAIR LOSS  If it can, it's never been scientifically proven, says Dr. Helene Rosenzweig, a dermatologist based in Encino, Calif.  Proponents of massage _ treatments typically involve rubbing an oil-based formula into the scalp _ claim a myriad of benefits, including an increase in circulation to the scalp, which causes the hair bulb to be flooded with the nutrients necessary for healthy hair growth.  And while dermatologists counter that the scalp is already one of the most vascularized areas of the body, they're admittedly not completely sure how to account for the fact that some placebo patients _ who rub onto their scalps a preparation that doesn't contain any active ingredient _ grow hair in controlled studies of minoxidil.  As long as the only harm from a scalp massage is to the wallet, Rosenzweig _ speculating that there may be a placebo effect or perhaps an anti-stress component _ doesn't discourage patients from having them. Just from expecting miracles. MYTH NO. 4: YOU CAN SHRINK A PORE  ``You can't change the size of your pores, but you can refine them,'' Rosenzweig says. While the size of pores is genetically determined, once you hit puberty, they can look enlarged if they become impacted with keratin, sebaceous material or bacteria.  Luckily, anti-agers such as Retin-A and alpha-hydroxy acids will give pores a good cleaning, thus making them appear smaller. MYTH NO. 5: DRY SKIN CAUSES WRINKLES  Not exactly. The reality is that most of the lines and wrinkles you see in the mirror were caused by the sun. The other 20 percent are the result of facial expressions such as smiling and frowning, both of which have lines named after them.  However, according to Dr. Susan Taylor, a Philadelphia-based dermatologist, ``The skin will appear more wrinkled when it's dehydrated.'' Conversely, the more sheen the skin has, the less noticeable wrinkling will be _ a definite advantage for oilier-skinned women.  A moisturizer will help smooth away crepiness caused by dryness, but _ unless it's formulated with an alpha-hydroxy acid _ it's not likely to make much headway on any sun-induced etchings. MYTH NO. 6: ALCOHOL-FREE IS BETTER  Hardly. ``When people see `alcohol,' they usually think of isopropyl, or rubbing alcohol,'' which imparts a cooling, drying sensation to the skin, says Dr. Mary Lupo, associate clinical professor of dermatology at Tulane University.  This, however, couldn't be further from the truth. From a biochemical viewpoint, she explains, alcohol just means that there's a molecular compound with an OH at the end of it. ``What's at the other end could completely alter its behavior in products.''  For example, cetyl, behenyl or oleyl alcohol are all so-called fatty alcohols, which act as emollients _ ingredients that decrease water loss from the skin, as well as soften it. MYTH NO. 7: EVERYBODY NEEDS A MOISTURIZER  There are no hard-and-fast rules about who needs a moisturizer. But according to Matarasso, you do if you experience the following clinical signs: redness, scaliness or itchiness _ all of which occur more frequently in cold weather.  As common sense would dictate, those with drier skin will get the most relief from a heavier moisturizer, while the mildly dry may want to stick with lighter lotions.  When does it make sense to have two different moisturizers? If you're extremely dry on, say, your hand and only moderately dry on your body, Taylor advises using separate products.  The best way to apply a moisturizer? Following a bath or shower after blotting the skin dry. Says Matarasso, ``If you put moisturizer on dry skin, all it's going to do is moisten your clothes _ it doesn't really stick to you.'' MYTH NO. 8: A SPLIT END CAN BE REPAIRED  Only if you're willing to snip the hair beyond the point of the split, says Dr. Paul Lazar, professor of clinical medicine at Northwestern University School of Medicine.  While some conditioners can temporarily ``glue'' split ends back together, there is no way to permanently reattach the hair shaft when the individual layers of cells separate _ the unfortunate outcome of too-frequent or too-harsh chemical processing, styling or just plain old washing.  Indeed, getting hair squeaky clean is actually an achievement that does more damage than good _ particularly to the ends of hair, which, having been around the longest, are most vulnerable to assault.  When shampooing, concentrate on cleansing the scalp, which is mostly where skin oil accumulates. As the shampoo is rinsed out, it will clean the length of the hair. MYTH NO. 9: IT'S TOO LATE TO START USING SUNSCREEN  It's never too late to start protecting skin from the sun. ``It's the cumulative effect of the sun that's damaging, so by avoiding it, you could halt some of its progress,'' Rosenzweig says.  Furthermore, there is clinical evidence that once you start protecting the skin, it has the ability to repair itself.  That's not to say, however, that it will happen overnight. As Janice Teal, director of Avon's skin-care product development, says, it's a slow, gradual process that can take a couple of years to yield significant changes.  But still, the idea that simply by starting to use a sunscreen a 40-year-old woman whose skin looked 50 could eventually have a complexion that looked its chronological age, is impressive. MYTH NO. 10: ONCE YOU HAVE A FACE-LIFT, YOU'LL NEED ANOTHER  This sounds like a myth that a lot of celebrities have taken at face value, but it has no basis in fact.  What is true is that a face-lift doesn't reverse the aging process. ``It just makes you look better than you would have looked without it from that point on,'' says Dr. Kenneth Rothaus, assistant clinical professor of surgery at New York Hospital-Cornell University Medical College.  As for whether you'd ever choose to do it again, it's really a personal choice.  According to Dr. Daniel C. Baker, associate professor of plastic surgery at New York University School of Medicine, it's not unusual for a woman who's had a face-lift to decide she still looks pretty good _ or at least better than all her friends _ and opt out of the second surgery.  (Karyn Repinski is the beauty and style director of Longevity magazine.) ------------------------------------------------- (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448, or by fax her at 816-822-1444.) Includes a 300-word sidebar and a color slide of a scene from ``Congo.'' (This ``separate buy'' article is from this week's PageUp Lifestyle and Entertainment Package.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) By RUSSELL ATWOOD c.1995 A&AMP;E Monthly Excerpted from A&AMP;E Monthly Distributed by New York Times Special Features  What's the one thing that terrifies you?  Is it snakes gliding across the ground? The scratching of scurrying rats? Are you afraid of the dark? Of tight, suffocating spaces? Of dizzying heights?  Whatever your fear, chances are Hollywood has filmed it because, while no one likes to be afraid, moviegoers the world over will pay to be scared.  A good horror movie (and sometimes even a bad one) can be as exhilarating as a roller-coaster ride, with sharp turns and deadly plummets taking your breath away and making your pulse race.  Horror comes in many sizes, from the giant predator T. Rex in 1993's ``Jurassic Park'' to the small ``whiteheaded fly'' trapped in a spider's web, pitifully screaming, ``Help me, help meeee!'' in ``The Fly'' (1958).  The degree of fright varies too, from the shock of a monster jumping out of the shadows to the chill that sneaks up on us late at night with the creak of a floorboard.  Whatever shape or size, whether real or imagined, all fears have one thing in common: the feeling of helplessness they provoke.  Helplessness is a sore spot for humankind.  As advanced as man has become, he is never far from those ancestors who shivered in the darkness, fearful of beasts and the elements. We've tamed the wild, obliterated superstition and built complex buildings, but one earthquake, one tornado or, in the case of ``King Kong'' (1933), one really big, ticked-off monkey can strip away that facade of superiority and leave us humbled.  ``Kong'' remains one of the greatest horror movies of all time, in large part because of the painstaking animation by special-effects pioneer Willis O'Brien.  Even armed with guns and bombs, man finds himself outmatched by Kong. That theme is still fresh today, evidenced by recent movies such as ``Jurassic Park'' and this year's ``Congo.''  Over the years man has also attempted to subdue the myths and legends that haunt his dreams, reducing creatures like vampires and werewolves to mere psychological explanations.  In ``The Wolf Man'' (1941), Lon Chaney Jr. plays a 20th-century man who arrogantly dismisses the existence of the supernatural. Chaney scoffs when he hears the legend of the werewolf: ``Even a man who is pure in heart and says his prayers by night can become a wolf when the wolfsbane blooms and the autumn moon is bright.''  Then he is bitten by one himself and the curse turns prophetic. The film's horror emanates not so much from Chaney's werewolf visage as from his inability to stop the dread transformation.  In his non-fiction book ``Danse Macabre'' (Berkeley Publishing, 1985), Stephen King asserts that the American horror movie can often serve as an ``accurate barometer of those things which trouble the night-thoughts of a whole society.''  This was never truer than in the 1950s in the aftermath of Hiroshima. Confronted with the reality of the Bomb, we started asking ourselves just what ``those scientists'' were doing and whether or not they should be doing it.  In ``Them'' (1954), ants, transformed by fallout into gigantic monsters, terrorize a small desert community, and an elderly scientist voices our collective fear that ``We have opened a door on an unimaginable power and there will be no closing it now.''  The other big fear of the '50s and '60s that found its way to the screen was the communist threat.  Much has been made of the ``red scare'' subtext of Don Siegel's ``Invasion of the Body Snatchers'' (1956), in which residents of a small town are being systematically replaced by soulless duplicates hatched from alien pods. But the film remains as chilling today as when it was released.  ``Body Snatchers'' is one of the first movies to demonstrate that as our society becomes more complex, so do our monsters.  The movie is set in Smalltown, U.S.A., bringing horror closer and closer to home. No longer did you have to go to a Transylvanian castle or participate in an archaeological dig in Egypt to meet up with monsters; they were there in your own backyard, maybe already in the house.  The history of horror movies can be seen as a limiting of places where we feel safe, and in 1960 Alfred Hitchcock launched an assault on what many consider the last refuge from intrusion: the bathroom.  The shower scene from ``Psycho'' (1960) reigns supreme in the annals of horror. It took Hitchcock seven days to shoot, using 70 camera setups, for 45 seconds of footage.  The scene opens with the victim (Janet Leigh) at her most vulnerable _ naked in a cascade of water _ and we watch, like bound and gagged voyeurs, as the shower curtain is yanked aside to reveal a flashing kitchen knife.  As the weapon begins its rampant descent, Leigh tries to ward off the blade with her upraised hands. But her actions are futile; she's as helpless as the viewers to stop the attack.  Over the years ``Psycho'' has served as the model for countless imitations, but none can touch the original.  The same is true of Hitchcock's next foray into horror, ``The Birds'' (1963), which established the premise that, given half a chance, nature will rise up and dispense with humankind without hesitation. The film chronicles a mass attack on a California community by our fine feathered friends.  It's a movie that inspired a slew of imitations, pitting man against everything from bloodthirsty rabbits (``Night of the Lepus,'' 1972) to killer worms (``Squirm,'' 1976).  By the late 1960s moviegoers had come face to face with all sorts of monsters, but in ``Night of the Living Dead'' (1968) the greatest monster was revealed to be ourselves.  A space probe to Venus returns to Earth bearing radiation that begins to revive the dead, not as we remember them, but as shuffling, mindless zombies intent on devouring the living.  Director George Romero's cult classic deserves its avid following; shot in black and white on a very low budget, the shoestring production values actually contribute to the authentic feel of the film.  The plot centers on seven people barricaded in a secluded farmhouse who, when they're not fighting the zombies, succumb to claustrophobia and fighting among themselves. In the end, the dead become less threatening than the living.  Two sequels (``Dawn of the Dead,'' 1978, and ``Day of the Dead,'' 1985) followed, but their visceral horror won't be to everyone's taste.  Another movie that you may want to think twice about seeing is William Friedkin's ``The Exorcist'' (1973).  While most horror movies lose their effect after the first viewing, ``The Exorcist'' is one shocker that sustains its impact no matter how many times you see it.  Much of the jolt comes from the taboo lines the film crosses regarding religion; whether you believe in God or not, by the end of the movie you have no trouble believing in the Devil.  The film also touches on society's concern about what is happening to its children. One moment 12-year-old Regan (Linda Blair) is the ideal little girl, the next she's croaking obscenities, spitting up green goop and spinning her head around.  Kids today, what're you going to do with them?  Parenting itself came under attack in the 1980s as people spoke more freely about child and spousal abuse. Horror movies reflected our collective fears about where the American family was headed.  No longer was home synonymous with safety. What use is it to lock the door when the leering maniac with the hatchet is the person closest to you?  In Stanley Kubrick's film adaptation of Stephen King's ``The Shining'' (1980), patriarch Jack Nicholson gets an extreme case of cabin fever while he and his family occupy an isolated hotel with a very nasty history.  Nicholson's descent from loving father to raving lunatic is masterful, and Shelley Duvall plays perfectly the dutiful wife making excuses for daddy's odd behavior.  A pivotal scene in the movie shows Duvall waking her husband from a nightmare and patting his back to soothe him, until he relates the dream: He killed her and his son and chopped them up into little pieces.  Duvall's hand freezes, afraid to touch him, as if he were a stranger.  Horror movies have made us question whether we're safe anywhere _ even in our dreams.  Wes Craven's ``A Nightmare on Elm Street'' (1984) set a standard which none of its imitators or numerous sequels has come close to matching.  From the very opening scene _ a pair of dirty hands forging a special glove with knives for fingers _ the viewer is sucked into a world of surreal images operating on the twisted logic of dreams. A dead girl opens her mouth to speak and a centipede slithers out; perfectly solid, carpeted steps turn to pools of goo when you try to climb them.  Like all great horror movies, ``Nightmare'' attacks our belief that everything is as it seems.  Fear, of course, has been around a lot longer than horror movies. It's something we carry with us all the time.  Watching horror movies, though, gives us the opportunity to take those fears out, examine them more closely and maybe even understand why we give them so much power. -0- SIDEBAR: CHEAP CHILLS  Sometimes not even the biggest budgets and most elaborate special effects can produce a good horror movie.  But very often the opposite proves true: Some of the best horror films have been made for next to nothing.  Here are some of my favorite creepy cheapies:  _ ``Carnival of Souls'' (1962). A low-budget, black-and-white classic, short on special effects but long on eerie atmosphere, about a woman _ the sole survivor of a car accident _ who is pursued by a ghostly figure.  The horror emanates from the woman's inability to escape the phantom and the gradual realization that maybe she didn't survive the car wreck after all.  _ ``Dementia 13'' (1963). While cheesy as a B-movie can get, this low-budget thriller about an ax murderer loose on a country estate in Ireland is still fun to watch because it marked Francis Ford Coppola's debut as a director.  _ ``The Conqueror Worm'' (1968). Vincent Price gives a chilling performance as the corrupt and sadistic witch-finder general, Matthew Hopkins (a real-life witch hunter during the 1600s), who torments two young lovers to the brink of madness.  An obscure movie that's hard to find on television or at the video store, but once you see it you won't forget its horrifying ending.  _ ``Halloween'' (1978). Notable as Jamie Lee Curtis' film debut, John Carpenter's seminal fright fest stands up well, thanks to the director's chilling music, clever camera angles and depiction of the killer as devoid of all compassion and reason.  _ ``Scanners'' (1981). An early film by director David Cronenberg (``The Fly,'' 1986 remake; ``Naked Lunch,'' 1991) about the locked-away powers of the mind and the people who hold the key. Most notable for its clever special effects and its famous scene of a man's head exploding. (Warning: Do not see this movie until one hour after eating!)  (Russell Atwood is assistant editor of A&AMP;E Monthly.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) ------------------------------------------------- </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19951016.0256 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (BC-TV'S-SINGLES-SCENE-PAGEUP-ART-NYTSF) </DATELINE> <TEXT> CHECKING OUT TV'S SINGLES SCENE (This ``separate buy'' article is from this week's PageUp Lifestyle and Entertainment Package.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) (She also has information on how to subscribe to PageUp, a weekly package of eight intriguing articles with color art.) Includes a color slide of Jonathan Silverman in a scene from ``The Single Guy.'' By ANITA GATES c.1995 The New York Times Distributed by New York Times Special Features  Jonathan, the title character of NBC's new series ``The Single Guy,'' does not seem to have an enviable life.  All of his close friends got married in the past few years, and he didn't. Now they're determined to save him from bachelorhood. (``You married people have this bizarre need to turn everyone else into married people,'' he says to one woman. ``You're like vampires. Or Mormons.'')  He's a novelist, but from the looks of his East Village apartment, not a wildly successful one. When he gets pay-per-view to watch a fight, the cable installer insults his television set and raids his refrigerator. Then he misses the fight because he's forced into a blind date with a name-dropping magazine editor who oinks when she laughs.  But Jonathan (played by Jonathan Silverman) shouldn't feel alone at all. Prime time is crawling with young, urban single people. They are the central characters in at least eight new sitcoms this season, and the casts of ``Seinfeld,'' ``Ellen'' and ``Friends'' are all back for another year of dating angst and wacky but unwavering friendship in the big city in the '90s.  So what's the big deal about being unmarried? Yes, ``Friends'' was a huge hit for NBC last year, but the new shows can't all be clones. Can they?  Brad Hall, the executive producer of ``The Single Guy'' (and best known for his stint on ``Saturday Night Live''), considers the phenomenon just the latest variation on the theme of the need to belong.  He sees it this way: the '60s, ``all those rural shows, belonging in a small town'' (``The Andy Griffith Show,'' ``Petticoat Junction''); the '70s, the workplace as family (``The Mary Tyler Moore Show,'' ``The Bob Newhart Show,'' ``Taxi''); the '80s, real families again (``The Cosby Show,'' ``Family Ties''); the '90s, a loving network of friends. (Hall's wife, Julia Louis-Dreyfus, appears on one of those '90s shows, as Elaine on ``Seinfeld.'')  ``People are always looking for a family unit of some kind, and TV is very cyclical,'' Hall says. ``It's just a need to be in a tribe and to look for support from your peers. And there's tons of comedy in that, because nobody is the same.''  The networks certainly hope that's true. Even the fledgling WB network has an entry, ``First Time Out,'' about young singles trying to make it in Los Angeles.  The question is: Who's watching all these shows?  When the producers of ``Friends'' were trying to sell it to NBC in the spring of 1994, the target audience was no secret.  ``It skews very young,'' said Leslie Moonves, then the president of Warner Bros. Television, which produces the series.  And Kevin Bright, an executive producer, said the show was about ``the time of everyone's life when they're at the beginning of everything.''  As ``Friends'' enters its second year, almost everybody seems to be watching.  ``It has a very broad demographic appeal,'' says Janine Jones, NBC's director of prime-time series publicity. ``The core audience is 18 to 54, but it does branch out and pull in teen viewers.''  NBC hasn't checked into how many of the show's fans are single and how many are married.  ``My feeling is that it offers something for everyone: people who are excited to get to that point in life, people who are at that point and people who can look back and reflect on it,'' Jones says. ``It's an exciting time in someone's life. It's really all about finding yourself and learning about yourself.''  Sounds great. But none of the new series seem to show the single life as particularly appealing.  On CBS's ``Can't Hurry Love,'' Nancy McKeon's character, Annie, a 28-year-old personnel placement interviewer, is described as earning ``under $20,000 a year'' (but somehow living alone in Manhattan) and not having ``slept with anyone in over seven months.''  She meets a handsome young police officer on the subway, but on their first date, the restaurant fireplace burns up her sweater and she loses interest when she learns he goes bird hunting.  Annie also has the bad luck to live across the hall from Jayne Mansfield's daughter, literally. The neighbor, Didi, who answers her apartment door with a supply of condoms in hand, is played by Mariska Hargitay, Mansfield's child by the body builder Mickey Hargitay.  Annie's best male friend, Roger (Louis Mandylor), takes his dates to the mattress department at Bloomingdale's.  There is some hope for love on the Fox series ``Too Something,'' but the characters' living conditions don't look any better.  Eric and Donny (Eric Schaeffer and Donal Lardner Ward) work in a corporate mail room while dreaming about other careers and take turns sleeping on the living room sofa because they've rented their second bedroom to a dog walker.  Eric and a very pretty M.B.A. at the office have eyes for each other. The men's friend Evelyn (Lisa Gerstein), however, is a typical lovelorn single; her boyfriend breaks up with her because he's moving to Budapest, but she soon learns that he only went as far away as Manhattan's Little Budapest (``York Avenue and 79th'').  Fox's other new series about single people, ``The Crew,'' focuses on a group of flight attendants based in Miami. Its uniqueness may lie in the fact that one of the attractive young singles is a black woman and one is a gay man.  On the first episode of ``The Drew Carey Show,'' no central characters come close to having a date. Drew (played by the show's namesake), a personnel interviewer at a Cleveland department store, is even dumped by his car pool. His neighbor Kate (Christa Miller) has just broken up with her live-in boyfriend.  At a press conference this summer, Carey, a stand-up comedian, was asked about the similarities between his show and last year's big sitcom hit.  ``Well, `Friends' is a show about young, good-looking people in their 20s,'' he said. ``And I'm in my 30s and I'm overweight and I'm not that good-looking. And I appreciate the comparison.''  Peter Noah, the creator and executive producer of ``Dweebs,'' on CBS, has a stronger reaction when his new show is compared to ``Friends.''  ``I feel as if we're being shoehorned into this year's premise,'' he says. ``Our characters are single and unattached, but this is not a show about dating or about the adventures of single people.''  The lead characters of ``Dweebs'' are brilliant nerds, partners in a high-tech firm, ``self-imposed exiles'' whose marital status, Noah adds, is ``reflective of their social isolation more than any particular trend.''  These are men with bad posture who have allergies and wear clothes ``the color of mulch.''  ``It's more of a traditional workplace show,'' Noah says, comparing ``Dweebs'' to the series ``Taxi.'' ``And there's a Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs quality to it.''  Snow White would be Carey (Farrah Forke), the small company's new computer-illiterate but socially normal office manager.  The hip, beautiful characters on ``Friends'' might not even speak to the pitiful people on some of this year's new series. But the great thing about all the television singles is their constant availability.  The gang on ``Friends'' seem never to spend an evening with anyone else, and they're always taking on projects as a group, like learning to play poker or stuffing envelopes with Rachel's (Jennifer Aniston's) resume.  And it's clear, from early episodes of several of the new shows, that today's single people stop off at one another's apartments on their way home from dates. Considering the extra subway ride or taxi fare _ and many of these shows are set in New York _ that is true friendship.  (Anita Gates in a New York Times staff writer.) (To publish this article, it must be purchased _ the rate is not prohibitive _ from New York Times Syndicate sales representative CONNIE WHITE in Kansas City at 1-800-444-0267 or 816-822-8448; fax: 816-822-1444.) "87440"	"he"	"explains"	" Using an electric needle cautery that seals tissue as it cuts, Toaff slices through the uterine peritoneum around the tumor. With a few strokes he sees what he is up against. ``This is not a fibroid,'' he says, squinting at the tissue. ``This is adenomyosis.'' If it were a fibroid, he explains, the border between the uterine wall and the tumor would be distinct, and the fibroid would slip out like a seed from a grape. His e-mail address is jholley(at)express-news.net </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20021018.0111 type=multi > <HEADLINE> YOUR PLACE: WHAT'S NEW IN HOME AND GARDEN </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> 10-18-02 14:23:00EDT (BC-YOUR-PLACE-HOME-BRIEF) </DATELINE> <TEXT>  FROM FRAME TO CANVAS: A picture speaks a thousand words, but with Photowow.com it can shout a million. The Los Angeles-based business turns ordinary snapshots into works of art.  ``When I moved out here six years ago from Florida, I had a barren white wall in my apartment that was perfect to hang art on,'' says Robert Schiff, founder of Photowow.com. But Schiff found the cost of larger, quality art to be $4,000 or more. The sticker shock led him to try using his old photographs, but imaging companies argued that the process was too intense and in the end would be too expensive. ``How can I make this a business?'' Schiff asked himself. It was from these obstacles that he began to realize the potential of Photowow.com.  The company can put your baby's face in a Warhol-style masterpiece or turn your parents' wedding photo into a painting with vintage hand-tint. Photowow.com offers 40 different designs and 15 different types of products, including paintings, pillows and T-shirts.  The process is simple. Take your photos to any of three stores -- in Encino, Los Angeles or Valencia. While there you can decide exactly what you want. In about a week, a proof e-mail will be sent to you for your approval. If you like it, your final print will be delivered in about a week. If not, then you'll discuss changes and the process will start again. You can also follow the same steps either via mail or the Internet.  Prices depend on the style and size. The most popular, the Andy Warhol one-panel pop, starts around $150 and varies depending on options. Until Nov. 3, 10 percent of the proceeds from personalized, custom-framed Warhol-style works of art will be donated to UCLA to provide scholarships for students in the Department of Design Media Arts. To find out more, call (800) 453-9333 or visit the Web site at www.photowow.com. -- Addison Pate  UPDATING AMERICA: Old houses have found a new niche. Instead of being updated and modernized, homes are being lovingly restored by their owners. In California, one of the most popular designs is the bungalow, noted for its low, sheltered porch but often having a second story and numerous additions to make room for married families and their children. To learn more about the bungalow, and how to restore yours, see ``Bungalows'' (Taunton Press; $29.95) by M. Caren Connolly and Louis Wasserman. Don't have a bungalow? Stay tuned. Future books in the new ``Updating Classic America'' series include ``Capes,'' ``Colonials'' and ``Ranches,'' which will be published in 2003. -- B.D.  COUNTRY DIGS: Southern California is known mostly for post-World War II tract houses, but the land was once ranches and orchards, so you'll still find a few farmhouses and red barns in areas such as Granada Hills and Ventura. Whether they've got Victorian or Cape Cod influences, or look more like a saltbox, farmhouses date back to America's roots, evoking warm and cozy feelings that have been captured in Leah Rosch's nostalgic coffee table book ``American Farmhouses: Country Style and Design'' (Simon &amp; Schuster; $35). The author, who is former editor of American Homestyle &amp; Gardening magazine, is a New Yorker who pays tribute to farm life with more than 200 nostalgic photos, by Keith Scott Morton, of both the houses and their barns. Rosch even tackles the question of why barns are painted red. Farmers, she explains, discovered that wood lasted longer with paint, and the only ``paint'' they had was from readily available ingredients, a mix of iron oxide and cows' milk -- which turned a brick red. -- B.D. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20021018.0112 type=story > <HEADLINE> THE REAL DIRT: GARDEN DESIGNERS OPEN UP ABOUT WHAT THEY LOVE AND </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> 10-18-02 14:25:00EDT (BC-LOVE-HATE-GARDENING-L) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200210.gz"
